title="Finno-Ugric peoples">Finno-Ugric peoples</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Sámi people</b> (also spelled <b>Sami</b> or <b>Saami</b>) are an indigenous <a href="/wiki/Finno-Ugric_peoples" title="Finno-Ugric peoples">Finno-Ugric people</a> inhabiting <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1pmi" title="Sápmi">Sápmi</a>, which today encompasses large northern parts of <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, northern parts of <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Kola_Peninsula" title="Kola Peninsula">Kola Peninsula</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Murmansk_Oblast" title="Murmansk Oblast">Murmansk Oblast</a> of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. The Sámi have historically been known in English as <b>Lapps</b> or <b>Laplanders.</b> Sámi ancestral lands are not well-defined. Their traditional languages are the <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_languages" title="Sámi languages">Sámi languages</a> and are classified as a branch of the <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic</a> <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">language family</a>.
</p><p>Traditionally, the Sámi have pursued a variety of livelihoods, including coastal fishing, fur trapping, and <a href="/wiki/Shepherd" title="Shepherd">sheep herding</a>. Their best-known means of livelihood is semi-<a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a> herding. Currently about 10% of the Sámi are connected to reindeer herding, providing them with meat, fur, and transportation. 2,800 Sámi people are actively involved in reindeer herding on a full-time basis.<sup id="cite_ref-galdu.org_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-galdu.org-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> For traditional, environmental, cultural, and political reasons, reindeer herding is legally reserved for only Sámi people in some regions of the Nordic countries.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymologies">Etymologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1pmi" title="Sápmi">Sápmi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fran%C3%A7ois-Auguste_Biard_-_Un_Lapon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Fran%C3%A7ois-Auguste_Biard_-_Un_Lapon.jpg/220px-Fran%C3%A7ois-Auguste_Biard_-_Un_Lapon.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Fran%C3%A7ois-Auguste_Biard_-_Un_Lapon.jpg/330px-Fran%C3%A7ois-Auguste_Biard_-_Un_Lapon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Fran%C3%A7ois-Auguste_Biard_-_Un_Lapon.jpg/440px-Fran%C3%A7ois-Auguste_Biard_-_Un_Lapon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2443" data-file-height="3200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fran%C3%A7ois-Auguste_Biard_-_Un_Lapon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Laplander depicted in art, painting by <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois-Auguste_Biard" title="François-Auguste Biard">François-Auguste Biard</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Sámi refer to themselves as <i lang="smi" title="Sami languages collective text">Sámit</i> (the Sámis) or <i lang="smi" title="Sami languages collective text">Sápmelaš</i> (of Sámi kin), the word <i lang="smi" title="Sami languages collective text">Sámi</i> being <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_inflection" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammatical inflection">inflected</a> into various grammatical forms. It has been proposed that Sámi (presumably <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">borrowed</a> from the Proto-Finnic word), <i lang="fiu" title="Finno-Ugrian languages collective text">Häme</i> (Finnish for '<a href="/wiki/Tavastia_(historical_province)" title="Tavastia (historical province)">Tavastia</a>') (← <a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Proto-Finnic</a> &#42;<i lang="fiu" title="Finno-Ugrian languages collective text">šämä</i>, the second <i>ä</i> still being found in the archaic derivation <i lang="fi" title="Finnish language text">Häm<b>ä</b>läinen</i>), and perhaps <i lang="fi" title="Finnish language text">Suomi</i> (Finnish for 'Finland') (← &#42;<i lang="fiu" title="Finno-Ugrian languages collective text">sōme-</i>/<i lang="fiu" title="Finno-Ugrian languages collective text">sōma-</i>, compare <i lang="fi" title="Finnish language text">suom<b>a</b>lainen</i>, supposedly borrowed from a <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a> source &#42;<i lang="gem" title="Germanic languages collective text">sōma-</i> from <a href="/wiki/Baltic_languages" title="Baltic languages">Proto-Baltic</a> &#42;<i lang="bat" title="Baltic languages collective text">sāma-</i>, in turn borrowed from Proto-Finnic &#42;<i lang="fiu" title="Finno-Ugrian languages collective text">šämä</i>) are of the same origin and ultimately borrowed from the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_languages" title="Baltic languages">Baltic</a> word &#42;<i lang="bat" title="Baltic languages collective text">žēmē</i>, meaning 'land'.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The Baltic word is <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a> with <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic</a> <i lang="sla-Latn" title="Slavic languages collective text">zemlja</i> (<span lang="sla-Cyrl" title="Slavic languages collective text">земля</span>), which also means 'land'.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The Sámi institutions – notably the <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_parliaments" class="mw-redirect" title="Sámi parliaments">parliaments</a>, radio and TV stations, theatres, etc. – all use the term <i>Sámi</i>, including when addressing outsiders in Norwegian, Swedish, Finnish, or English. In Norwegian, the Sámi are today referred to by the Norwegianized form <i lang="no" title="Norwegian language text">Same</i>.
</p><p>The first probable historical mention of the Sámi, naming them <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Fenni</i>, was by <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>, about AD&#8201;98.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Variants of <i>Finn</i> or <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Fenni</i> were in wide use in ancient times, judging from the names <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Fenni" title="Fenni">Fenni</a></i> and <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Φίννοι</span> (<i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text"><a href="/wiki/Phinnoi" title="Phinnoi">Phinnoi</a></i>) in classical <a href="/wiki/Latin_literature" title="Latin literature">Roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_literature" title="Ancient Greek literature">Greek works</a>. <i>Finn</i> (or variants, such as <i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">skridfinn</i>, 'striding Finn') was the name originally used by Norse speakers (and their proto-Norse speaking ancestors) to refer to the Sámi, as attested in the Icelandic <a href="/wiki/Eddas" class="mw-redirect" title="Eddas">Eddas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norse_sagas" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse sagas">Norse sagas</a> (11th to 14th centuries). The etymology is somewhat uncertain, but the consensus seems to be that it is related to Old Norse <i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">finna</i>, from proto-Germanic &#42;<i lang="gem" title="Germanic languages collective text">finthanan</i> ('to find'),<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> the logic being that the Sámi, as <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherers" class="mw-redirect" title="Hunter-gatherers">hunter-gatherers</a> "found" their food, rather than grew it. As Old Norse gradually developed into the separate Scandinavian languages, Swedes apparently took to using <i>Finn</i> to refer to inhabitants of what is now Finland, while the Sámi came to be called <i>Lapps</i>. In Norway, however, Sámi were still called <i>Finns</i> at least until the modern era (reflected in toponyms like <i lang="no" title="Norwegian language text"><a href="/wiki/Finnmark" title="Finnmark">Finnmark</a></i>, <i lang="no" title="Norwegian language text"><a href="/wiki/Finnsnes" title="Finnsnes">Finnsnes</a></i>, <i lang="no" title="Norwegian language text">Finnfjord</i> and <i lang="no" title="Norwegian language text">Finnøy</i>), and some northern Norwegians will still occasionally use <i>Finn</i> to refer to Sámi people, although the Sámi themselves now consider this to be an inappropriate term. Finnish immigrants to Northern Norway in the 18th and 19th centuries were referred to as <a href="/wiki/Kven_people" title="Kven people">Kvens</a> to distinguish them from the Sámi "Finns". Ethnic <a href="/wiki/Finns" title="Finns">Finns</a> (<i lang="fi" title="Finnish language text">Suomi</i>) are a distinct group from Sámi.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lapp">Lapp</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Lapp">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Laureus,_Lappalaisia_nuotiolla_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Laureus%2C_Lappalaisia_nuotiolla_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Laureus%2C_Lappalaisia_nuotiolla_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Laureus%2C_Lappalaisia_nuotiolla_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Laureus%2C_Lappalaisia_nuotiolla_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Laureus%2C_Lappalaisia_nuotiolla_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Laureus%2C_Lappalaisia_nuotiolla_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1102" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Laureus,_Lappalaisia_nuotiolla_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aleksander_Laur%C3%A9us" title="Aleksander Lauréus">Aleksander Lauréus</a>'s painting of the Sámi by the fire</div></div></div>
<p>The word <i>Lapp</i> can be traced to <a href="/wiki/Old_Swedish" title="Old Swedish">Old Swedish</a> <i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">lapper</i>, Icelandic <i lang="is" title="Icelandic language text">lappir</i> (plural) of Finnish origin; compare Finnish <i lang="fi" title="Finnish language text">lappalainen</i> "Lapp", <i>Lappi</i> "Lapland" (possibly meaning "wilderness in the north"), the original meaning being unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> It is unknown how the word <i>Lapp</i> came into the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Norse language</a>, but one of the first written mentions of the term is in the <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Gesta_Danorum" title="Gesta Danorum">Gesta Danorum</a></i> by 12th-century Danish historian <span lang="la" style="font-style:normal;" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Saxo_Grammaticus" title="Saxo Grammaticus">Saxo Grammaticus</a></span>, who referred to <i>the two Lappias</i>, although he still referred to the Sámi as <i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">(Skrid-)Finn</i>s.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In fact, Saxo never explicitly connects the Sámi with the "two Laplands". The term "Lapp" was popularized and became the standard terminology by the work of <span lang="la" style="font-style:normal;" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Johannes_Schefferus" title="Johannes Schefferus">Johannes Schefferus</a></span>, <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Lapponia_(book)" title="Lapponia (book)">Acta Lapponica</a></i> (1673).
</p><p>The Sámi are often known in other languages by the <a href="/wiki/Exonym" class="mw-redirect" title="Exonym">exonyms</a> <i>Lap</i>, <i>Lapp</i>, or <i>Laplanders</i>, although these are considered derogatory terms,<sup id="cite_ref-Paine57_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paine57-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> while others accept at least the name <i>Lappland</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sámis_don’t_want_to_be_Lapps_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sámis_don’t_want_to_be_Lapps-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Variants of the name <i>Lapp</i> were originally used in Sweden and Finland and, through Swedish, adopted by many major European languages: English: <span lang="en">Lapps</span>; German, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>: <i lang="nl">Lappen</i>; <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Lapons</i>; <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">Λάπωνες</span> (<i lang="el-Latn" title="Greek language text">Lápōnes</i>); <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a>: <i lang="hu">lappok</i>; <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <i lang="it">Lapponi</i>; <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>: <i lang="pl">Lapończycy</i>; <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>: <i lang="pt">Lapões</i>; <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>: <i lang="es">Lapones</i>; <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a>: <i lang="ro">laponi</i>; <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>: <i lang="tr">Lapon</i>. In Russian the corresponding term is <span lang="ru" title="Russian language text">лопари́</span> (<i lang="ru-Latn" title="Russian language text">lopari</i>) and in Ukrainian <span lang="uk" title="Ukrainian language text">лопар◌́і́</span> (<i lang="uk-Latn" title="Ukrainian language text">lopari</i>).
</p><p>In Finland and Sweden, <i>Lapp</i> is common in place names, such as <i>Lappi</i> (<span lang="fi" style="font-style:normal;" title="Finnish language text"><a href="/wiki/Satakunta" title="Satakunta">Satakunta</a></span>), <i lang="fi" title="Finnish language text">Lappeenranta</i> (<a href="/wiki/South_Karelia" title="South Karelia">South Karelia</a>) and <i lang="fi" title="Finnish language text">Lapinlahti</i> (<a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Savo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="North Savo (page does not exist)">North Savo</a>) in Finland; and <i>Lapp</i> (<a href="/wiki/Stockholm_County" title="Stockholm County">Stockholm County</a>), <i>Lappe</i> (<span lang="sv" style="font-style:normal;" title="Swedish language text"><a href="/wiki/S%C3%B6dermanland" title="Södermanland">Södermanland</a></span>) and <i lang="sv" title="Swedish language text">Lappabo</i> (<span lang="sv" style="font-style:normal;" title="Swedish language text"><a href="/wiki/Sm%C3%A5land" title="Småland">Småland</a></span>) in Sweden. As already mentioned, <i>Finn</i> is a common element in Norwegian (particularly Northern Norwegian) place names, whereas <i>Lapp</i> is exceedingly rare.
</p><p>Terminological issues in Finnish are somewhat different. Finns living in <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Lapland" class="mw-redirect" title="Finnish Lapland">Finnish Lapland</a> generally call themselves <i lang="fi" title="Finnish language text">lapp<b>i</b>lainen</i>, whereas the similar word for the Sámi people is <i lang="fi" title="Finnish language text">lapp<b>a</b>lainen</i>. This can be confusing for foreign visitors because of the similar lives Finns and Sámi people live today in Lapland. <i lang="fi" title="Finnish language text">Lappalainen</i> is also a common family name in Finland. In Finnish, <i lang="fi" title="Finnish language text">saamelainen</i> is the most commonly used word nowadays, especially in official contexts.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sami_history" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami history">Sami history</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LocationSapmi.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/LocationSapmi.png/220px-LocationSapmi.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/LocationSapmi.png/330px-LocationSapmi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/LocationSapmi.png/440px-LocationSapmi.png 2x" data-file-width="811" data-file-height="789" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LocationSapmi.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Homeland of the Sámi people</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saami_Family_1900.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Saami_Family_1900.jpg/220px-Saami_Family_1900.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Saami_Family_1900.jpg/330px-Saami_Family_1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Saami_Family_1900.jpg/440px-Saami_Family_1900.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3320" data-file-height="2440" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saami_Family_1900.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Sámi family in Norway around 1900</div></div></div>
<p>The Sámi people of Arctic Europe have lived and worked in an area that stretches over the northern parts of the regions currently known as Norway, Sweden, Finland, and the Russian <a href="/wiki/Kola_Peninsula" title="Kola Peninsula">Kola Peninsula</a>. They have inhabited the northern arctic and sub-arctic regions of <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a> for 3,500 years.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Before that they were probably living in the Finno-Ugric homeland.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The Sámi are counted among the Arctic peoples and are members of circumpolar groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Council" title="Arctic Council">Arctic Council Indigenous Peoples' Secretariat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Petroglyphs and archeological findings such as settlements dating from about 10,000&#160;BC can be found in Lapland and <a href="/wiki/Finnmark" title="Finnmark">Finnmark</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-http_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-http-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> These hunters and gatherers of the late <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> were named <a href="/wiki/Komsa" class="mw-redirect" title="Komsa">Komsa</a> by the researchers, as what they identified themselves as is unknown.
</p><p>The geographical distribution of the Sámi has evolved over the course of history. The Sámi populations most likely lived in the <a href="/wiki/Ural_Mountains" title="Ural Mountains">Ural Mountains</a> region,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> which is the origin of the <a href="/wiki/Finno-Ugric_languages" title="Finno-Ugric languages">Finno-Ugric languages</a>, of which the <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_languages" title="Sámi languages">Sámi languages</a> are part, in addition to the presence of <a href="/wiki/Nganasan_people" title="Nganasan people">Nganasan</a> DNA up to plus or minus 25%<sup id="cite_ref-:0_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> and clearly <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_Siberia" title="Indigenous peoples of Siberia">Siberian</a> <a href="/wiki/Y_chromosome" title="Y chromosome">Y chromosome</a> <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup" title="Haplogroup">haplogroup</a> confirms this theory.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> From the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> the Sámi occupied the area off the coast of Finnmark and the Kola peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_22-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> It is at the same time that the arrival of the Siberian genome is dated in <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> and Finland, which probably corresponds to the introduction of the Finno-Ugric languages in the region<sup id="cite_ref-:0_22-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Southern_limits_of_S.C3.A1mi_settlement_in_the_past"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_limits_of_Sámi_settlement_in_the_past">Southern limits of Sámi settlement in the past</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Southern limits of Sámi settlement in the past">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lapper_og_Reinsdyr.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Lapper_og_Reinsdyr.jpg/220px-Lapper_og_Reinsdyr.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Lapper_og_Reinsdyr.jpg/330px-Lapper_og_Reinsdyr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Lapper_og_Reinsdyr.jpg/440px-Lapper_og_Reinsdyr.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="758" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lapper_og_Reinsdyr.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Sámi man and child in Finnmark, Norway, circa 1900</div></div></div>
<p>How far south the Sami extended in the past has been debated among historians and archeologists for many years. The Norwegian historian <a href="/wiki/Yngvar_Nielsen" title="Yngvar Nielsen">Yngvar Nielsen</a>, commissioned by the Norwegian government in 1889 to determine this question to settle contemporary questions of Sámi land rights, concluded that the Sámi had lived no farther south than <a href="/wiki/Lierne" title="Lierne">Lierne</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nord-Tr%C3%B8ndelag" title="Nord-Trøndelag">Nord-Trøndelag</a> county until around 1500, when they started moving south, reaching the area around Lake <a href="/wiki/Femund" title="Femund">Femund</a> in the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> This hypothesis is still accepted among many historians, but has been the subject of scholarly debate in the 21st century. In recent years, several archaeological finds indicate a Sámi presence in southern Norway in the Middle Ages, and southern Sweden,<sup id="cite_ref-Broadbent_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broadbent-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> including finds in <a href="/wiki/Lesja" title="Lesja">Lesja</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Vang,_Oppland" title="Vang, Oppland">Vang</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Valdres" title="Valdres">Valdres</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Hol" title="Hol">Hol</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%85l" title="Ål">Ål</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hallingdal" title="Hallingdal">Hallingdal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gjerde_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gjerde-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Proponents of the Sámi interpretations of these finds assume a mixed population of Norse and Sámi people in the mountainous areas of southern Norway in the Middle Ages.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Origins_of_the_Norwegian_Sea_S.C3.A1mi"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_of_the_Norwegian_Sea_Sámi">Origins of the Norwegian Sea Sámi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Origins of the Norwegian Sea Sámi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Three_S%C3%A1mi_Lapp_women,_c1890s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Three_S%C3%A1mi_Lapp_women%2C_c1890s.jpg/220px-Three_S%C3%A1mi_Lapp_women%2C_c1890s.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Three_S%C3%A1mi_Lapp_women%2C_c1890s.jpg/330px-Three_S%C3%A1mi_Lapp_women%2C_c1890s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Three_S%C3%A1mi_Lapp_women%2C_c1890s.jpg/440px-Three_S%C3%A1mi_Lapp_women%2C_c1890s.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1296" data-file-height="1314" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Three_S%C3%A1mi_Lapp_women,_c1890s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Three Sámi women</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bubonic_plague">Bubonic plague</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Bubonic plague">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1928_Lyngen_Troms_Norway_group_Mountain_Sami_people_Photo_pcard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/1928_Lyngen_Troms_Norway_group_Mountain_Sami_people_Photo_pcard.jpg/220px-1928_Lyngen_Troms_Norway_group_Mountain_Sami_people_Photo_pcard.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/1928_Lyngen_Troms_Norway_group_Mountain_Sami_people_Photo_pcard.jpg/330px-1928_Lyngen_Troms_Norway_group_Mountain_Sami_people_Photo_pcard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/1928_Lyngen_Troms_Norway_group_Mountain_Sami_people_Photo_pcard.jpg/440px-1928_Lyngen_Troms_Norway_group_Mountain_Sami_people_Photo_pcard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2980" data-file-height="1961" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1928_Lyngen_Troms_Norway_group_Mountain_Sami_people_Photo_pcard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sámi people in Norway, 1928</div></div></div>
<p>Until the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a> in northern Norway in 1349, the Sámi and the Norwegians occupied very separate economic <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">niches</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The Sámi hunted reindeer and fished for their livelihood. The Norwegians, who were concentrated on the outer islands and near the mouths of the <a href="/wiki/Fjords" class="mw-redirect" title="Fjords">fjords</a>, had access to the major European trade routes so that, in addition to marginal farming in the <a href="/wiki/Nordland" title="Nordland">Nordland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Troms" title="Troms">Troms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Finnmark" title="Finnmark">Finnmark</a> counties, they were able to establish commerce, supplying fish in trade for products from the south.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The two groups co-existed using two different food resources.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> According to old Nordic texts, the Sea Sámi and the Mountain Sámi are two classes of the same people and not two different ethnic groups, as had been erroneously believed.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This social economic balance greatly changed when <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a> came to northern Norway in December 1349. The Norwegians were closely connected to the greater European trade routes, along which the plague traveled; consequently, they were infected and died at a far higher rate than Sámi in the interior. Of all the states in the region, Norway suffered the most from this <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plague</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Depending on the <a href="/wiki/Parish" title="Parish">parish</a>, sixty to seventy-six percent of the northern Norwegian farms were abandoned following the plague,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> while land-rents, another possible measure of the population numbers, dropped down to 9–28% of pre-plague rents.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Although the population of northern Norway is sparse compared to southern Europe, the disease spread just as fast.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The method of movement of the plague-infested <a href="/wiki/Flea" title="Flea">flea</a> <i>(<a href="/wiki/Xenopsylla_cheopsis" class="mw-redirect" title="Xenopsylla cheopsis">Xenopsylla cheopsis</a>)</i> from the south was in wooden <a href="/wiki/Barrels" class="mw-redirect" title="Barrels">barrels</a> holding wheat, rye, or wool, where the fleas could live, and even reproduce, for several months at a time.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The Sámi ate fish and reindeer meat, and did not eat <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a>. They lived in communities detached from the Norwegians, and being only weakly connected to the European trade routes, they fared far better than the Norwegians.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fishing_industry">Fishing industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Fishing industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sj%C3%B8samisk_Mann_Finnmark_Norge_Ivar_Samuelsen_1884_av_Bonaparte.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Sj%C3%B8samisk_Mann_Finnmark_Norge_Ivar_Samuelsen_1884_av_Bonaparte.jpg/170px-Sj%C3%B8samisk_Mann_Finnmark_Norge_Ivar_Samuelsen_1884_av_Bonaparte.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Sj%C3%B8samisk_Mann_Finnmark_Norge_Ivar_Samuelsen_1884_av_Bonaparte.jpg/255px-Sj%C3%B8samisk_Mann_Finnmark_Norge_Ivar_Samuelsen_1884_av_Bonaparte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Sj%C3%B8samisk_Mann_Finnmark_Norge_Ivar_Samuelsen_1884_av_Bonaparte.jpg/340px-Sj%C3%B8samisk_Mann_Finnmark_Norge_Ivar_Samuelsen_1884_av_Bonaparte.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1029" data-file-height="1812" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sj%C3%B8samisk_Mann_Finnmark_Norge_Ivar_Samuelsen_1884_av_Bonaparte.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Sea Sámi man from <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> by <a href="/wiki/Prince_Roland_Bonaparte" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Roland Bonaparte">Prince Roland Bonaparte</a> in 1884</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sea_sami_man.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Sea_sami_man.jpg/170px-Sea_sami_man.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/df/Sea_sami_man.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="243" data-file-height="369" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sea_sami_man.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Sea Sámi man from Norway by <a href="/wiki/Prince_Roland_Bonaparte" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Roland Bonaparte">Prince Roland Bonaparte</a> in 1884</div></div></div>
<p>Fishing has always been the main livelihood for the many Sámi living permanently in coastal areas.<sup id="cite_ref-un.org_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-un.org-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Archeological research shows that the Sámi have lived along the coast and once lived much farther south in the past, and they were also involved in work other than reindeer herding (e.g., fishing, agriculture, iron work).<sup id="cite_ref-Broadbent_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broadbent-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The fishing along the north Norwegian coast, especially in the Lofoten and Vesterålen islands, is quite productive with a variety of fish, and during medieval times, it was a major source of income for both the fisherman and the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_monarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian monarchy">Norwegian monarchy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> With such massive population drops caused by the Black Death, the tax revenues from this industry greatly diminished. Because of the huge economic profits that could be had from these fisheries, the local authorities offered incentives to the Sámi—faced with their own population pressures—to settle on the newly vacant farms.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>  This started the economic division between the Sea Sámi (<i>sjøsamene</i>), who fished extensively off the coast, and the Mountain Sámi (<i>fjellsamene, innlandssamene</i>), who continued to hunt reindeer and small-game animals. They later herded reindeer. Even as late as the early 18th century, there were many Sámi who were still settling on these farms left abandoned from the 1350s.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> After many years of continuous migration, these Sea Sámi became far more numerous than the reindeer mountain Sámi, who today only make up 10% of all Sámi. In contemporary times, there are also ongoing consultations between the Government of Norway and the Sámi Parliament regarding the right of the coastal Sámi to fish in the seas on the basis of historical use and international law.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> State regulation of sea fisheries underwent drastic change in the late 1980s. The regulation linked quotas to vessels and not to fishers. These newly calculated quotas were distributed free of charge to larger vessels on the basis of the amount of the catch in previous years, resulting in small vessels in Sámi districts falling outside the new quota system to a large degree.<sup id="cite_ref-un.org_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-un.org-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johansen_2010_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johansen_2010-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Mountain_S.C3.A1mi"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mountain_Sámi">Mountain Sámi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mountain Sámi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As the Sea Sámi settled along Norway's fjords and inland waterways, pursuing a combination of farming, cattle raising, trapping and fishing, the minority Mountain Sámi continued to hunt wild <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a>. Around 1500, they started to tame these animals into herding groups, becoming the well-known reindeer nomads, often portrayed by outsiders as following the archetypal Sámi lifestyle. The Mountain Sámi had to pay taxes to three states, <a href="/wiki/Kalmar_Union" title="Kalmar Union">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Early_Vasa_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Vasa era">Sweden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Moscow" title="Grand Duchy of Moscow">Russia</a>, as they crossed each border while following the annual reindeer migrations; this caused much resentment over the years.<sup id="cite_ref-Kuiper_1990s_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuiper_1990s-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Between 1635–1659, the Swedish crown forced Swedish conscripts and Sámi cart drivers to work at a mine at <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nasafj%C3%A4ll&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Nasafjäll (page does not exist)">Nasafjäll</a>, causing many Sámis to emigrate from the area to avoid forced labour. As a result, the population of Pite and Lule Sámis decreased greatly.<sup id="cite_ref-Kuiper_1990s_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuiper_1990s-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-1800s">Post-1800s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Post-1800s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22S%C3%A1mi+people%22">"Sámi people"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22S%C3%A1mi+people%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22S%C3%A1mi+people%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22S%C3%A1mi+people%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22S%C3%A1mi+people%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22S%C3%A1mi+people%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2011</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>For long periods of time, the Sami lifestyle thrived because of its adaptation to the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> environment. Indeed, throughout the 18th century, as Norwegians of Northern Norway suffered from low fish prices and consequent depopulation, the Sami cultural element was strengthened, since the Sami were mostly independent of supplies from Southern Norway.
</p><p>During the 19th century, the pressure of <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Scandinavia" title="Christianization of Scandinavia">Christianization</a> of the Sami increased, with some Sami adopting <a href="/wiki/Laestadianism" title="Laestadianism">Laestadianism</a>. With the introduction of seven compulsory years of school in 1889, the Sami language and traditional way of life came increasingly under pressure from forced cultural normalization. Sami people were occasionally subjected to eugenics through forced sterilization from 1934, during the reign of the social democrat <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(Norway)" title="Labour Party (Norway)">Labour Party</a>. Strong economic development of the north also ensued, giving Norwegian culture and language higher status.
</p><p>On the Swedish and Finnish sides, the authorities were less militant, although the Sami language was forbidden in schools and strong economic development in the north led to weakened cultural and economic status for the Sami. From 1913 to 1920, the Swedish race-segregation political movement created a race-based biological institute that collected research material from living people and graves, and sterilized Sami women. Throughout history, Swedish settlers were encouraged to move to the northern regions through incentives such as land and water rights, tax allowances, and military exemptions.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The strongest pressure took place from around 1900 to 1940, when Norway invested considerable money and effort to wipe out Sami culture. Anyone who wanted to buy or lease state lands for agriculture in <a href="/wiki/Finnmark" title="Finnmark">Finnmark</a> had to prove knowledge of the Norwegian language and had to register with a Norwegian name. This caused the <a href="/wiki/Dislocation_of_Sami_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Dislocation of Sami people">dislocation of Sami people</a> in the 1920s, which increased the gap between local Sami groups (something still present today) that sometimes has the character of an internal Sami ethnic conflict. In 1913, the Norwegian parliament passed a bill on "native act land" to allocate the best and most useful lands to Norwegian settlers. Another factor was the <a href="/wiki/Scorched_earth" title="Scorched earth">scorched earth</a> policy conducted by the German army, resulting in heavy <a href="/wiki/Lapland_War" title="Lapland War">war destruction</a> in northern Finland and northern Norway in 1944–45, destroying all existing houses, or <i>kota</i>, and visible traces of Sami culture. After <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> the pressure was relaxed, though the legacy was evident into recent times, such as the 1970s law limiting the size of any house Sami people were allowed to build.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Alta_controversy" title="Alta controversy">controversy</a> over the construction of the hydro-electric power station in <a href="/wiki/Alta,_Norway" title="Alta, Norway">Alta</a> in 1979 brought Sami rights onto the political agenda. In August 1986, the national anthem ("<a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_soga_l%C3%A1vlla" class="mw-redirect" title="Sámi soga lávlla">Sámi soga lávlla</a>") and flag (<a href="/wiki/Sami_flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami flag">Sami flag</a>) of the Sami people were created. In 1989, the first Sami parliament in Norway was elected. In 2005, the <a href="/wiki/Finnmark_Act" title="Finnmark Act">Finnmark Act</a> was passed in the <a href="/wiki/Storting" title="Storting">Norwegian parliament</a> giving the Sami parliament and the Finnmark Provincial council a joint responsibility of administering the land areas previously considered state property. These areas (96% of the provincial area), which have always been used primarily by the Sami, now belong officially to the people of the province, whether Sami or Norwegian, and not to the Norwegian state.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary">Contemporary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Contemporary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The indigenous Sámi population is a mostly urbanised demographic, but a substantial number live in villages in the high arctic. The Sámi are still coping with the cultural consequences of language and culture loss related to generations of Sámi children taken to missionary and/or state-run boarding schools and the legacy of laws that were created to deny the Sámi rights (e.g., to their beliefs, language, land and to the practice of traditional livelihoods). The Sámi are experiencing cultural and environmental threats,<sup id="cite_ref-Korpijaakko-Mikkel_March_22,_2009_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korpijaakko-Mikkel_March_22,_2009-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>  including oil exploration, mining, dam building, logging, climate change, military bombing ranges, tourism and commercial development.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Syterskalet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Syterskalet.jpg/220px-Syterskalet.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Syterskalet.jpg/330px-Syterskalet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Syterskalet.jpg/440px-Syterskalet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2032" data-file-height="1354" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Syterskalet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vindelfjällen</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural-resource_prospecting">Natural-resource prospecting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Natural-resource prospecting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Sapmi is rich in precious metals, oil,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and natural gas. Mining activities in Arctic Sapmi cause controversy when they are in grazing and calving areas. Mining projects are rejected by the Sami Parliament in the Finnmark area. The Sami Parliament demands that resources and mineral exploration should benefit mainly the local Sami communities and population, as the proposed mines are in Sami lands and will affect their ability to maintain their traditional livelihood.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Mining locations even include ancient Sami spaces that are designated as ecologically protected areas, such as the <a href="/wiki/Vindelfj%C3%A4llen_Nature_Reserve" title="Vindelfjällen Nature Reserve">Vindelfjällen Nature Reserve</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In Russia's Kola Peninsula, vast areas have already been destroyed by mining and smelting activities, and further development is imminent. This includes oil and natural gas exploration in the Barents Sea. There is a gas pipeline that stretches across the Kola Peninsula. Oil spills affect fishing and the construction of roads. Power lines may cut off access to reindeer calving grounds and sacred sites.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining">Mining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Kallak_mine" title="Kallak mine">Kallak</a> (Sámi: <i>Gállok</i>) a group of indigenous and non-indigenous activists protested to stop the UK-based mining company <a href="/wiki/Beowulf_Mining" title="Beowulf Mining">Beowulf</a> from carrying on a drilling program in reindeer winter grazing lands.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> There is often local opposition to new mining projects where environmental impacts are perceived to be very large. New modern mines eliminate the need for many types of jobs and new job creation.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> ILO Convention No. 169 would grant rights to the Sámi people to their land and give them power in matters that affect their future.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Swedish taxes on minerals are low in an international comparison in order to increase mineral exploration. There are also few plans for mine reclamation.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Logging">Logging</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Logging">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In northern Finland, there has been a longstanding dispute over the destruction of forests, which prevents reindeer from migrating between seasonal feeding grounds and destroys supplies of lichen that grow on the upper branches of older trees. This lichen is the reindeer's only source of sustenance during the winter months, when snow is deep. The logging has been under the control of the state-run forest system.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Greenpeace, reindeer herders, and Sámi organisations carried out a historic joint campaign, and in 2010, Sámi reindeer herders won some time as a result of these court cases. Industrial logging has now been pushed back from the most important forest areas either permanently or for the next 20 years, though there are still threats, such as mining and construction plans of holiday resorts on the protected shorelines of Lake Inari.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_activities">Military activities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Military activities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Government authorities and NATO have built bombing-practice ranges in Sámi areas in northern Norway and Sweden. These regions have served as reindeer calving and summer grounds for thousands of years, and contain many ancient Sámi sacred sites.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_rights">Land rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Land rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Suorvajaure_in_stora_sjofallet_park.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Suorvajaure_in_stora_sjofallet_park.jpg/220px-Suorvajaure_in_stora_sjofallet_park.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Suorvajaure_in_stora_sjofallet_park.jpg/330px-Suorvajaure_in_stora_sjofallet_park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Suorvajaure_in_stora_sjofallet_park.jpg/440px-Suorvajaure_in_stora_sjofallet_park.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2961" data-file-height="1962" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Suorvajaure_in_stora_sjofallet_park.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suorvajaure&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Suorvajaure (page does not exist)">Suorvajaure</a> near <a href="/wiki/Pite%C3%A5" title="Piteå">Piteå</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Swedish government has allowed the world's largest onshore wind farm to be built in Piteå, in the Arctic region where the Eastern Kikkejaure village has its winter reindeer pastures. The wind farm will consist of more than 1,000 wind turbines and an extensive road infrastructure, which means that the feasibility of using the area for winter grazing in practice is impossible. Sweden has received strong international criticism, including by the UN Racial Discrimination Committee and the Human Rights Committee, that Sweden violates Sami <i>landrättigheter</i> (land rights), including by not regulating industry. In Norway some Sami politicians (for example – Aili Keskitalo) suggest giving the Sami Parliament a special veto right on planned mining projects.<sup id="cite_ref-barents_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barents-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_rights">Water rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Water rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>State regulation of sea fisheries underwent drastic change in the late 1980s. The regulation linked quotas to vessels and not to fishers. These newly calculated quotas were distributed free of cost to larger vessels on the basis of the amount of the catch in previous years, resulting in small vessels in Sámi districts falling outside the new quota system to a large degree.
</p><p>The Sámi recently stopped a water-prospecting venture that threatened to turn an ancient sacred site and natural spring called Suttesaja into a large-scale water-bottling plant for the world market—without notification or consultation with the local Sámi people, who make up 70 percent of the population. The Finnish National Board of Antiquities has registered the area as a heritage site of cultural and historical significance, and the stream itself is part of the Deatnu/Tana watershed, which is home to Europe's largest salmon river, an important source of Sámi livelihood.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Norway, government plans for the construction of a hydroelectric power plant in the Alta river in Finnmark in northern Norway led to a political controversy and the rallying of the Sámi popular movement in the late 1970s and early 1980s. As a result, the opposition in the <a href="/wiki/Alta_controversy" title="Alta controversy">Alta controversy</a> brought attention to not only environmental issues but also the issue of Sámi rights.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change_and_environment">Climate change and environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Climate change and environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nordkappsami.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Nordkappsami.jpg/220px-Nordkappsami.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Nordkappsami.jpg/330px-Nordkappsami.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Nordkappsami.jpg/440px-Nordkappsami.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1977" data-file-height="1405" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nordkappsami.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sámi man from Norway</div></div></div>
<p>Reindeer have major cultural and economic significance for indigenous peoples of the North. The human-ecological systems in the North, like reindeer pastoralism, are sensitive to change, perhaps more than in virtually any other region of the globe, due in part to the variability of the Arctic climate and ecosystem and the characteristic ways of life of indigenous Arctic peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1986 <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_nuclear_disaster" class="mw-redirect" title="Chernobyl nuclear disaster">Chernobyl nuclear disaster</a> caused nuclear fallout in the sensitive Arctic ecosystems and poisoned fish, meat<sup id="cite_ref-Skuerud_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skuerud-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> and berries. Lichens and mosses are two of the main forms of vegetation in the Arctic and are highly susceptible to airborne pollutants and heavy metals. Since many do not have roots, they absorb nutrients, and toxic compounds, through their leaves. The lichens accumulated airborne radiation, and 73,000 reindeer had to be killed as "unfit" for human consumption in Sweden alone. The government promised Sámi indemnification, which was not acted upon by government.
</p><p>Radioactive wastes and spent nuclear fuel have been stored in the waters off the Kola Peninsula, including locations that are only "two kilometers" from places where Sámi live. There are a minimum of five "dumps" where spent nuclear fuel and other radioactive waste are being deposited in the Kola Peninsula, often with little concern for the surrounding environment or population.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The tourism industry in Finland has been criticized for turning Sami culture into a marketing tool by promoting opportunities to experience "authentic" Sámi ceremonies and lifestyle. At many tourist locales, non-Sámi dress in inaccurate replicas of Sámi traditional clothing, and gift shops sell crude reproductions of Sámi handicraft. One popular "ceremony", crossing the Arctic Circle, actually has no significance in Sámi spirituality. To the Sámi, this is an insulting display of cultural exploitation.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="box-POV plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-POV" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Unbalanced scales.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/45px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png" decoding="async" width="45" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/68px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/90px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="354" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">The <b><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutrality</a> of this article is <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NPOV_dispute" title="Wikipedia:NPOV dispute">disputed</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Relevant discussion may be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:S%C3%A1mi_people##" title="Talk:Sámi people">talk page</a>. Please do not remove this message until <a href="/wiki/Template:POV#When_to_remove" title="Template:POV">conditions to do so are met</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2019</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span id="Discrimination_against_the_S.C3.A1mi"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Discrimination_against_the_Sámi">Discrimination against the Sámi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Discrimination against the Sámi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Sámi have for centuries, even today, been the subject of discrimination and abuse by the dominant cultures claiming possession of their lands.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> They have never been a single community in a single region of Lapland, which until recently was considered only a cultural region.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Norway has been criticized internationally for the politics of <a href="/wiki/Norwegianization" title="Norwegianization">Norwegianization</a> of and discrimination against the aboriginal population of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> On 8 April 2011, the UN Racial Discrimination Committee recommendations were handed over to Norway; these addressed many issues, including the position of students needing bilingual education in Sámi. One committee recommendation was that no language be allowed to be a basis for discrimination in the Norwegian anti-discrimination laws, and it recommended wording of Racial Discrimination Convention Article 1 contained in the Act.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Give details of what Act – is this a Norwegian act or a reference to a United Nations provision? Add link (December 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Further points of recommendation concerning the Sámi population in Norway included the incorporation of the racial Convention through the Human Rights Act, improving the availability and quality of interpreter services, and equality of the civil Ombudsman's recommendations for action. A new present status report was to have been ready by the end of 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Even in Finland, where Sámi children, like all Finnish children, are entitled to day care and language instruction in their own language, the Finnish government has denied funding for these rights in most of the country, including even in Rovaniemi, the largest municipality in Finnish Lapland. Sámi activists have pushed for nationwide application of these basic rights.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As in the other countries claiming sovereignty over Sámi lands, Sámi activists' efforts in Finland in the 20th century achieved limited government recognition of the Sámis' rights as a recognized minority, but the Finnish government has maintained its legally enforced premise that the Sámi must prove their land ownership, an idea incompatible with and antithetical to the traditional reindeer-herding Sámi way of life. This has effectively allowed the Finnish government to take without compensation, motivated by economic gain, land occupied by the Sámi for centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Official_S.C3.A1mi_policy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_Sámi_policy">Official Sámi policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Official Sámi policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Norway">Norway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Norway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Samediggi03.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Samediggi03.jpg/220px-Samediggi03.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Samediggi03.jpg/330px-Samediggi03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Samediggi03.jpg/440px-Samediggi03.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Samediggi03.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_Parliament_of_Norway" title="Sámi Parliament of Norway">Sámi Parliament of Norway</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Sámi have been recognized as an <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Indigenous people">indigenous people</a> in Norway (1990 according to <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_and_Tribal_Peoples_Convention,_1989" title="Indigenous and Tribal Peoples Convention, 1989">ILO convention 169</a> as described below), and therefore, according to international law, the Sami people in Norway are entitled special protection and rights. The legal foundation of the Sámi policy is:<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Article 110a of the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian Constitution">Norwegian Constitution</a>.</li>
<li>The Sámi Act (act of 12 June 1987 No. 56 concerning the Sámi Parliament (the Sámediggi) and other legal matters pertaining to the Sámis).</li></ul>
<p>The constitutional amendment states: "It is the responsibility of the authorities of the State to create conditions enabling the Sámi people to preserve and develop its language, culture and way of life." This provides a legal and political protection of the Sámi language, culture and society. In addition the "amendment implies a legal, political and moral obligation for Norwegian authorities to create an environment conducive to the Sámis themselves influencing on the development of the Sámi community" (ibid.).
</p><p>The Sámi Act provides special rights for the Sámi people (ibid.):
</p>
<ul><li>"...&#160;the Sámis shall have their own national <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_Parliament_of_Norway" title="Sámi Parliament of Norway">Sámi Parliament</a> elected by and amongst the Sámis" (Chapter 1–2).</li>
<li>The Sámi people shall decide the area of activity of the Norwegian Sámi Parliament.</li>
<li>The Sámi and Norwegian languages have equal standing in Norway (section 15; Chapter 3 contains details with regards to the use of the Sámi language).</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:View_from_B%C3%A1rr%C3%A1s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/View_from_B%C3%A1rr%C3%A1s.jpg/220px-View_from_B%C3%A1rr%C3%A1s.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/View_from_B%C3%A1rr%C3%A1s.jpg/330px-View_from_B%C3%A1rr%C3%A1s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/View_from_B%C3%A1rr%C3%A1s.jpg/440px-View_from_B%C3%A1rr%C3%A1s.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3259" data-file-height="2440" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:View_from_B%C3%A1rr%C3%A1s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mountain landscape in <a href="/wiki/Kvalsund" title="Kvalsund">Kvalsund</a> near <a href="/wiki/Hammerfest" title="Hammerfest">Hammerfest</a></div></div></div>
<p>In addition, the Sámi have special rights to reindeer husbandry.
</p><p>The Norwegian Sámi Parliament also elects 50% of the members to the board of the <a href="/wiki/Finnmark_Estate" class="mw-redirect" title="Finnmark Estate">Finnmark Estate</a>, which controls 95% of the land in the county of <a href="/wiki/Finnmark" title="Finnmark">Finnmark</a>.
</p><p>Norway has also accepted international conventions, declarations and agreements applicable to the Sámi as a minority and indigenous people including:<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The International Covenant on Civil and Political Right (1966). Article 27 protects minorities, and indigenous peoples, against discrimination: "In those states in which ethnic, religious or linguistic minorities exist, persons belonging to such minorities, shall not be denied the right, in community with the other members of their group, to enjoy their own culture, to profess and practice their own religion, or use their own language."</li>
<li>ILO Convention No. 169 concerning Indigenous and Tribal Peoples in Independent Countries (1989). The convention states that rights for the indigenous peoples to land and natural resources are recognized as central for their material and cultural survival. In addition, indigenous peoples should be entitled to exercise control over, and manage, their own institutions, ways of life and economic development in order to maintain and develop their identities, languages and religions, within the framework of the states in which they live.</li>
<li>The International Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination (1965).</li>
<li>The UN Convention on the Rights of the Child (1989).</li>
<li>The UN Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination against Women (1979).</li>
<li>The Council of Europe's Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities (1995).</li>
<li>The Council of Europe's Charter for Regional and Minority Languages (1992).</li>
<li>The UN Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples (2007).<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>In 2007, the Norwegian Parliament passed the new Reindeer Herding Act acknowledging <a href="/wiki/Siida" title="Siida">siida</a> as the basic institution regarding land rights, organization, and daily herding management.<sup id="cite_ref-Korpijaakko-Mikkel_March_22,_2009_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korpijaakko-Mikkel_March_22,_2009-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">Sweden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Sweden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sametinget_-_Sami_Parliament_of_Sweden.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Sametinget_-_Sami_Parliament_of_Sweden.JPG/220px-Sametinget_-_Sami_Parliament_of_Sweden.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Sametinget_-_Sami_Parliament_of_Sweden.JPG/330px-Sametinget_-_Sami_Parliament_of_Sweden.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Sametinget_-_Sami_Parliament_of_Sweden.JPG/440px-Sametinget_-_Sami_Parliament_of_Sweden.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sametinget_-_Sami_Parliament_of_Sweden.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Sámi Parliament">Sámi Parliament</a> in Sweden</div></div></div>
<p>The Sametingslag was established as the Swedish Sámi Parliament as of 1 January 1993. Sweden recognised the existence of the "Sámi nation" in 1989, but the ILO Indigenous and Tribal Peoples Convention, C169 has not been adopted.
</p><p>The Compulsory School Ordinance states that Sámi pupils are entitled to be taught in their native language; however, a municipality is only obliged to arrange mother-tongue teaching in Sámi if a suitable teacher is available and the pupil has a basic knowledge of Sámi.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, after 14 years of negotiation, Laponiatjuottjudus, an association with Sámi majority control, will govern the <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a> <a href="/wiki/Laponian_area" title="Laponian area">Laponia</a>. The reindeer-herding law will apply in the area as well.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1998, Sweden formally apologized for the wrongs committed against the Sámi.
</p><p>Sámi is one of five national minority languages recognized by Swedish law.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Finland">Finland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Finland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yllastunturi_Finland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Yllastunturi_Finland.jpg/220px-Yllastunturi_Finland.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="59" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Yllastunturi_Finland.jpg/330px-Yllastunturi_Finland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Yllastunturi_Finland.jpg/440px-Yllastunturi_Finland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5564" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yllastunturi_Finland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Land near <a href="/wiki/Yll%C3%A4s" title="Ylläs">Ylläs</a></div></div></div>
<p>The act establishing the Finnish Sámi Parliament (Finnish: Saamelaiskäräjät) was passed on November 9, 1973.
Finland recognized the Sámi as a "people" in 1995, but they have yet to ratify ILO Convention 169 Concerning Indigenous and Tribal Peoples.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Lapland,_Finland-fi.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Map_of_Lapland%2C_Finland-fi.svg/220px-Map_of_Lapland%2C_Finland-fi.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Map_of_Lapland%2C_Finland-fi.svg/330px-Map_of_Lapland%2C_Finland-fi.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Map_of_Lapland%2C_Finland-fi.svg/440px-Map_of_Lapland%2C_Finland-fi.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="776" data-file-height="897" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Lapland,_Finland-fi.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lapland_(Finland)" title="Lapland (Finland)">Finnish Lapland</a>. The three northernmost municipalities Utsjoki, Inari and Enontekiö and part of Sodankylä is officially considered the Sámi area.</div></div></div>
<p>Finland ratified the 1966 <a href="/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Civil_and_Political_Rights" title="International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights">U.N. Covenant on Civil and Political Rights</a> though several cases have been brought before the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Human_Rights_Committee" title="United Nations Human Rights Committee">U.N. Human Rights Committee</a>. Of those, 36 cases involved a determination of the rights of individual Sámi in Finland and Sweden. The committee decisions clarify that Sámi are members of a minority within the meaning of Article 27 and that deprivation or erosion of their rights to practice traditional activities that are an essential element of their culture do come within the scope of Article 27.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The case of J.
