Lansman versus Finland concerned a challenge by Sámi reindeer herders in northern Finland to the Finnish Central Forestry Board's plans to approve logging and construction of roads in an area used by the herdsmen as winter pasture and spring calving grounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Finland Sámi have had access to Sámi language instruction in some schools since the 1970s, and language rights were established in 1992. There are three Sámi languages spoken in Finland: North Sámi, Skolt Sámi and Inari Sámi. Of these languages, Inari Sámi, which is spoken by about 350 speakers, is the only one that is used entirely within the borders of Finland, mainly in the municipality of Inari.
</p><p>Finland has denied any aboriginal rights or land rights to the Sámi people;<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> in Finland, non-Sámi can herd reindeer.
</p><p>Sámi people have had very little representation in Finnish national politics. In fact, as of 2007, <a href="/wiki/Janne_Seuruj%C3%A4rvi" title="Janne Seurujärvi">Janne Seurujärvi</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Centre_Party_(Finland)" title="Centre Party (Finland)">Finnish Centre Party</a> representative, was the first Sámi ever to be elected to the Finnish Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kildin_Saami_map.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Kildin_Saami_map.JPG/220px-Kildin_Saami_map.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/Kildin_Saami_map.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="299" data-file-height="242" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kildin_Saami_map.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kildin Sami Map. <a href="/w/index.php?title=%D0%A1%D0%90%D0%90%D0%9C%D0%98&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="СААМИ (page does not exist)">СААМИ</a><sup class="noprint" style="font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%A1%D0%B0%D0%B0%D0%BC%D1%8B" class="extiw" title="ru:Саамы">ru</a>&#93;</sup> is "Sámi" in Cyrillic</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lovozero_Center.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Lovozero_Center.JPG/220px-Lovozero_Center.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Lovozero_Center.JPG/330px-Lovozero_Center.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Lovozero_Center.JPG/440px-Lovozero_Center.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2288" data-file-height="1712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lovozero_Center.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>National Culture Center in <a href="/wiki/Lovozero_(rural_locality)" title="Lovozero (rural locality)">Lovozero</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Russia has not adopted the ILO Indigenous and Tribal Peoples Convention, C169. During the Soviet times the inhabitants of the Kola tundra were forcibly relocated to <a href="/wiki/Kolkhoz" title="Kolkhoz">kolkhoz'es</a> (collective communities) by the state;<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> most Saami were settled at Lujávri (<a href="/wiki/Lovozero_(rural_locality)" title="Lovozero (rural locality)">Lovozero</a>).
</p><p>The 1822 Statute of Administration of Non-Russians in Siberia asserted state ownership over all the land in Siberia and then "granted" possessory rights to the natives.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Governance of indigenous groups, and especially collection of taxes from them, necessitated protection of indigenous peoples against exploitation by traders and settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1993 Constitution, Article 69 states, "The Russian Federation guarantees the rights of small indigenous peoples in accordance with the generally accepted principles and standards of international law and international treaties of the Russian Federation."<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> For the first time in Russia, the rights of indigenous minorities were established in the 1993 Constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Russian Federation ratified the 1966 U.N. Covenant on Civil and Political Rights;<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Section 2 explicitly forbids depriving a people of "its own means of subsistence."<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The Russian parliament (Duma) has adopted partial measures to implement it.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Russian Federation lists distinct indigenous peoples as having special rights and protections under the Constitution and federal laws and decrees.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> These rights are linked to the category known since Soviet times as the <i>malochislennye narody</i> ("small-numbered peoples"), a term that is often translated as "indigenous minorities", which include Arctic peoples such as the Sámi, <a href="/wiki/Nenets_people" title="Nenets people">Nenets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evenks" title="Evenks">Evenki</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_people" title="Chukchi people">Chukchi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 1999, the Russian Duma passed a law that guarantees socio-economic and cultural development to all indigenous minorities, protecting traditional living places and acknowledging some form of limited ownership of territories that have traditionally been used for hunting, herding, fishing, and gathering activities. The law, however, does not anticipate the transfer of title in fee simply to indigenous minorities. The law does not recognize development rights, some proprietary rights including compensation for damage to the property, and limited exclusionary rights. It is not clear, however, whether protection of nature in the traditional places of inhabitation implies a right to exclude conflicting uses that are destructive to nature or whether they have the right to veto development.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Russian Federation's Land Code reinforces the rights of numerically small peoples ("indigenous minorities") to use places they inhabit and to continue traditional economic activities without being charged rent.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Such lands cannot be allocated for unrelated activities (which might include oil, gas, and mineral development or tourism) without the consent of the indigenous peoples. Furthermore, indigenous minorities and ethnic groups are allowed to use environmentally protected lands and lands set aside as nature preserves to engage in their traditional modes of land use.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Regional law, Code of the <a href="/wiki/Murmansk_Oblast" title="Murmansk Oblast">Murmansk Oblast</a>, calls on the organs of state power of the oblast to facilitate the native peoples of the Kola North, specifically naming the Sámi, "in realization of their rights for preservation and development of their native language, national culture, traditions and customs." The third section of Article 21 states: "In historically established areas of habitation, Sámi enjoy the rights for traditional use of nature and [traditional] activities."<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D1%83%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%B3%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8C.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/%D0%9C%D1%83%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%B3%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8C.jpg/220px-%D0%9C%D1%83%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%B3%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8C.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/%D0%9C%D1%83%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%B3%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8C.jpg/330px-%D0%9C%D1%83%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%B3%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8C.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/%D0%9C%D1%83%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%B3%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8C.jpg/440px-%D0%9C%D1%83%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%B3%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8C.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D1%83%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%B3%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8C.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Murmansk" title="Port of Murmansk">port of Murmansk</a> in the Kola Bay</div></div></div>
<p>Throughout the Russian North, indigenous and local people have difficulties with exercising control over resources upon which they and their ancestors have depended for centuries. The failure to protect indigenous ways, however, stems not from inadequacy of the written law, but rather from the failure to implement existing laws. Unfortunately, violations of the rights of indigenous peoples continue, and oil, gas, and mineral development and other activities, (mining, timber cutting, commercial fishing, and tourism) that bring foreign currency into the Russian economy, prevail over the rule of law.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chibini_massif_and_lake_imandra.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Chibini_massif_and_lake_imandra.jpg/220px-Chibini_massif_and_lake_imandra.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Chibini_massif_and_lake_imandra.jpg/330px-Chibini_massif_and_lake_imandra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Chibini_massif_and_lake_imandra.jpg/440px-Chibini_massif_and_lake_imandra.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="803" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chibini_massif_and_lake_imandra.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chibini massif, <a href="/wiki/Kola_Peninsula" title="Kola Peninsula">Kola Peninsula</a></div></div></div>
<p>The life ways and economy of indigenous peoples of the Russian North are based upon reindeer herding, fishing, terrestrial and sea mammal hunting, and trapping. Many groups in the Russian Arctic are semi-nomadic, moving seasonally to different hunting and fishing camps. These groups depend upon different types of environment at differing times of the year, rather than upon exploiting a single commodity to exhaustion.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Throughout northwestern Siberia, oil and gas development has disturbed pastureland and undermined the ability of indigenous peoples to continue hunting, fishing, trapping, and herding activities. Roads constructed in connection with oil and gas exploration and development destroy and degrade pastureland,<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> ancestral burial grounds, and sacred sites and increase hunting by oil workers on the territory used by indigenous peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%89%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B5.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%89%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B5.jpg/220px-%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%89%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B5.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%89%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B5.jpg/330px-%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%89%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%89%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B5.jpg/440px-%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%89%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B5.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%89%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B5.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Krasnoshchelye" title="Krasnoshchelye">Krasnoshchelye</a> village on the Ponoi River</div></div></div>
<p>In the Sámi homeland on the Kola Peninsula in northwestern Russia, regional authorities closed a fifty-mile (eighty-kilometer) stretch of the Ponoi River (and other rivers) to local fishing and granted exclusive fishing rights to a commercial company offering catch-and-release fishing to sport fishers largely from abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> This deprived the local Sámi (see Article 21 of the Code of the Murmansk Oblast) of food for their families and community and of their traditional economic livelihood. Thus, closing the fishery to locals may have violated the test articulated by the U.N. Human Rights Committee and disregarded the Land Code, other legislative acts, and the 1992 Presidential decree. Sámi are not only forbidden to fish in the eighty-kilometer stretch leased to the Ponoi River Company but are also required by regional laws to pay for licenses to catch a limited number of fish outside the lease area. Residents of remote communities have neither the power nor the resources to demand enforcement of their rights. Here and elsewhere in the circumpolar north, the failure to apply laws for the protection of indigenous peoples leads to "criminalization" of local indigenous populations who cannot survive without "poaching" resources that should be accessible to them legally.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although indigenous leaders in Russia have occasionally asserted indigenous rights to land and resources, to date there has been no serious or sustained discussion of indigenous group rights to ownership of land.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nordic">Nordic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Nordic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 16 November 2005 in <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a>, a group of experts, led by former Chief Justice of the Supreme Court of Norway Professor Carsten Smith, submitted a proposal for a Nordic Sámi Convention to the annual joint meeting of the ministers responsible for Sámi affairs in Finland, Norway and Sweden and the presidents of the three Sámi Parliaments from the respective countries. This convention recognizes the Sámi as one indigenous people residing across national borders in all three countries. A set of minimum standards is proposed for the rights of developing the Sámi language and culture and rights to land and water, livelihoods and society.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The convention has not yet been ratified in the Nordic countries.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sami_culture"><span id="Culture"></span> Sami culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Sami culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>To make up for past suppression, the authorities of Norway, Sweden and Finland now make an effort to build up Sámi cultural institutions and promote Sámi culture and language.
</p>
<h3><span id="Duodji_.28craft.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Duodji_(craft)">Duodji (craft)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Duodji (craft)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Duodji" title="Duodji">Duodji</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_knives_-_Arctic_Museum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Sami_knives_-_Arctic_Museum.jpg/220px-Sami_knives_-_Arctic_Museum.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Sami_knives_-_Arctic_Museum.jpg/330px-Sami_knives_-_Arctic_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Sami_knives_-_Arctic_Museum.jpg/440px-Sami_knives_-_Arctic_Museum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2784" data-file-height="1856" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_knives_-_Arctic_Museum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sámi knives</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_belt_and_needle_cases.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Sami_belt_and_needle_cases.JPG/220px-Sami_belt_and_needle_cases.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Sami_belt_and_needle_cases.JPG/330px-Sami_belt_and_needle_cases.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Sami_belt_and_needle_cases.JPG/440px-Sami_belt_and_needle_cases.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1051" data-file-height="606" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_belt_and_needle_cases.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beaded belt, knife, and antler needlecase</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_woman_with_white_reindeer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Sami_woman_with_white_reindeer.jpg/170px-Sami_woman_with_white_reindeer.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Sami_woman_with_white_reindeer.jpg/255px-Sami_woman_with_white_reindeer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Sami_woman_with_white_reindeer.jpg/340px-Sami_woman_with_white_reindeer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1572" data-file-height="2372" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_woman_with_white_reindeer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sámi woman from <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a></div></div></div>
<p>Duodji, the Sámi handicraft, originates from the time when the Sámis were self-supporting nomads, believing therefore that an object should first and foremost serve a purpose rather than being primarily decorative. Men mostly use wood, bone, and antlers to make items such as antler-handled <a href="/wiki/Scrimshaw" title="Scrimshaw">scrimshawed</a> <a href="/wiki/Sami_knife" title="Sami knife">sami knives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sami_drum" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami drum">drums</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guksi" title="Guksi">guksi</a> (burl cups). Women used leather and roots to make items such as <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A1kti" title="Gákti">gákti</a> (clothing), and birch- and spruce-root woven baskets.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clothing">Clothing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Clothing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A1kti" title="Gákti">Gákti</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Four_Winds_hat" title="Four Winds hat">Four Winds hat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beaska" title="Beaska">Beaska</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Luhkka" title="Luhkka">Luhkka</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_clothing_3.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Sami_clothing_3.JPG/220px-Sami_clothing_3.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Sami_clothing_3.JPG/330px-Sami_clothing_3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Sami_clothing_3.JPG/440px-Sami_clothing_3.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_clothing_3.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sámi hats</div></div></div>
<p>Gakti are the traditional clothing worn by the Sámi people. The gákti is worn both in ceremonial contexts and while working, particularly when herding reindeer.
</p><p>Traditionally, the gákti was made from reindeer leather and sinews, but nowadays, it is more common to use wool, cotton, or silk. Women's gákti typically consist of a dress, a fringed shawl that is fastened with 1–3 silver brooches, and boots/shoes made of reindeer fur or leather. Boots can have pointed or curled toes and often have band-woven ankle wraps. Eastern Sámi boots have a rounded toe on reindeer-fur boots, lined with felt and with beaded details. There are different gákti for women and men; men's gákti have a shorter "jacket-skirt" than a women's long dress. Traditional gákti are most commonly in variations of red, blue, green, white, medium-brown tanned leather, or reindeer fur. In winter, there is the addition of a reindeer fur coat and leggings, and sometimes a poncho (luhkka) and rope/lasso.
</p><p>The colours, patterns and the jewellery of the gákti indicate where a person is from, if a person is single or married, and sometimes can even be specific to their family. The collar, sleeves and hem usually have appliqués in the form of geometric shapes. Some regions have ribbonwork, others have tin embroidery, and some Eastern Sámi have beading on clothing or collar. Hats vary by sex, season, and region. They can be wool, leather, or fur. They can be embroidered, or in the East, they are more like a beaded cloth crown with a shawl. Some traditional shamanic headgear had animal hides, plaits, and feathers, particularly in East Sapmi.
</p><p>The gákti can be worn with a belt; these are sometimes band-woven belts, woven, or beaded. Leather belts can have scrimshawed antler buttons, silver concho-like buttons, tassles, or brass/copper details such as rings. Belts can also have beaded leather pouches, antler needle cases, accessories for a fire, copper rings, amulets, and often a carved and/or scrimshawed antler handled knife. Some Eastern Sámi also have a hooded jumper (малиц) from reindeer skins with wool inside and above the knee boots.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_and_literature">Media and literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Media and literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_media" title="Sámi media">Sámi media</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Johan_Turi,_Muitalus_s%C3%A1miid_birra.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Johan_Turi%2C_Muitalus_s%C3%A1miid_birra.jpg/220px-Johan_Turi%2C_Muitalus_s%C3%A1miid_birra.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Johan_Turi%2C_Muitalus_s%C3%A1miid_birra.jpg/330px-Johan_Turi%2C_Muitalus_s%C3%A1miid_birra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Johan_Turi%2C_Muitalus_s%C3%A1miid_birra.jpg/440px-Johan_Turi%2C_Muitalus_s%C3%A1miid_birra.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1471" data-file-height="1363" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Johan_Turi,_Muitalus_s%C3%A1miid_birra.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Muitalus sámiid birra</i> by <a href="/wiki/Johan_Turi" title="Johan Turi">Johan Turi</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li>There are short daily news bulletins in Northern Sámi on national TV in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>. <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_television" class="mw-redirect" title="Children&#39;s television">Children's television</a> shows in Sámi are also frequently made. There is also a radio station for Northern Sámi, which has some news programs in the other <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_languages" title="Sámi languages">Sámi languages</a>.</li>
<li>A single daily newspaper is published in Northern Sámi, <i><a href="/wiki/%C3%81vvir" title="Ávvir">Ávvir</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> along with a few magazines.</li>
<li>There is a Sámi theatre, <a href="/wiki/Beaivv%C3%A1%C5%A1_S%C3%A1mi_Theatre" class="mw-redirect" title="Beaivváš Sámi Theatre">Beaivvaš</a>, in Kautokeino on the Norwegian side, as well as in Kiruna on the Swedish side. Both tour the entire Sámi area with drama written by Sámi authors or international translations.</li>
<li>A number of novels and poetry collections are published every year in Northern Sámi, and sometimes in the other Sámi languages as well. The largest Sami Publishing house is Davvi Girji.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_music" title="Sámi music">Sámi music</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Riddu_Ri%C4%91%C4%91u_(9).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Riddu_Ri%C4%91%C4%91u_%289%29.jpg/170px-Riddu_Ri%C4%91%C4%91u_%289%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Riddu_Ri%C4%91%C4%91u_%289%29.jpg/255px-Riddu_Ri%C4%91%C4%91u_%289%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Riddu_Ri%C4%91%C4%91u_%289%29.jpg/340px-Riddu_Ri%C4%91%C4%91u_%289%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1488" data-file-height="1984" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Riddu_Ri%C4%91%C4%91u_(9).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sara Marielle Gaup at <a href="/wiki/Riddu_Ri%C4%91%C4%91u" title="Riddu Riđđu">Riddu Riđđu</a></div></div></div>
<p>A characteristic feature of Sámi musical tradition is the singing of <i><a href="/wiki/Joik" title="Joik">yoik</a></i>. Yoiks are song-chants and are traditionally sung <i><a href="/wiki/A_cappella" title="A cappella">a cappella</a></i>, usually sung slowly and deep in the throat with apparent emotional content of sorrow or anger. Yoiks can be dedicated to animals and birds in nature, special people or special occasions, and they can be joyous, sad or melancholic. They often are based on syllablic improvisation. In recent years, musical instruments frequently accompany yoiks. The only traditional Sámi instruments that were sometimes used to accompany yoik are the "fadno" flute (made from reed-like Angelica archangelica stems) and hand drums (frame drums and bowl drums).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Education with Sámi as the first language is available in all four countries, and also outside the Sámi area.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_University_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Sámi University College">Sámi University College</a> is located in Kautokeino. Sámi language is studied in several universities in all countries, most notably the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Troms%C3%B8" title="University of Tromsø">University of Tromsø</a>, which considers Sámi a mother tongue, not a foreign language.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals_and_markets">Festivals and markets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Festivals and markets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Numerous Sámi festivals throughout the Sápmi area celebrate different aspects of the Sámi culture. The best known on the Norwegian side is <a href="/wiki/Riddu_Ri%C4%91%C4%91u" title="Riddu Riđđu">Riddu Riđđu</a>, though there are others, such as <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ijahis_Idja&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ijahis Idja (page does not exist)">Ijahis Idja</a> in <a href="/wiki/Inari,_Finland" title="Inari, Finland">Inari</a>. Among the most festive are the Easter festivals taking place in <a href="/wiki/Kautokeino" title="Kautokeino">Kautokeino</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karasjok" title="Karasjok">Karasjok</a> prior to the springtime reindeer migration to the coast. These festivals combine traditional culture with modern phenomena such as snowmobile races.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_arts">Visual arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Visual arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In addition to Duodji (Sámi handicraft), there is a developing area of contemporary Sámi visual art. Galleries such as Sámi Dáiddaguovddáš (Sami Center for Contemporary Art)<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> are being established.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dance">Dance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Dance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For many years there was a misconception that the Sámi were the only Indigenous people without a dance tradition in the world.
<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="this citation seems nonexistent (March 2019)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
Sámi dance companies have emerged such as Kompani Nomad.
<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> 
A book about the "lost" Sámi dance tradition called <i>Jakten på den försvunna samiska dansen</i> was recently published by <a href="/wiki/Ume%C3%A5_University" title="Umeå University">Umeå University</a>'s Centre for Sami Research (CeSam).<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> In the eastern areas of Sápmi the dance tradition has been more continuous and is continued by groups such as Johtti Kompani.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reindeer_husbandry">Reindeer husbandry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Reindeer husbandry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reindeer#Reindeer_husbandry" title="Reindeer">Reindeer §&#160;Reindeer husbandry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Reindeer_herding.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Reindeer_herding.jpg/220px-Reindeer_herding.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Reindeer_herding.jpg/330px-Reindeer_herding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Reindeer_herding.jpg/440px-Reindeer_herding.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reindeer_herding.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reindeer <a href="/wiki/Herding" title="Herding">herding</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ljungris_July_2013.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Ljungris_July_2013.jpg/220px-Ljungris_July_2013.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Ljungris_July_2013.jpg/330px-Ljungris_July_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Ljungris_July_2013.jpg/440px-Ljungris_July_2013.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6016" data-file-height="4016" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ljungris_July_2013.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Building in <a href="/wiki/Berg_Municipality" title="Berg Municipality">Ljungris</a>, owned by the Sámi community and used especially for <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">Reindeer</a> calf marking in the summer.</div></div></div>
<p>Reindeer husbandry has been and still is an important aspect of Sámi culture. Traditionally the Sámi lived and worked in reindeer herding groups  called <i>siiddat</i>, which consisted of several families and their herds. Members of the <i>siidda</i> helped each other with the management and husbandry of the herds.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> During the years of <a href="/wiki/Forced_assimilation" title="Forced assimilation">forced assimilation</a>, the areas in which reindeer herding was an important livelihood were among the few where the Sami culture and language survived.
</p><p>Today in Norway and Sweden, reindeer husbandry is legally protected as an exclusive Sámi livelihood, such that only persons of Sámi descent with a linkage to a reindeer herding family can own, and hence make a living off, reindeer. Presently, about 2,800 people are engaged in reindeer herding in Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-galdu.org_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-galdu.org-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In Finland, reindeer husbandry is not exclusive and is practiced to a limited degree also by ethnic Finns. Legally, it is restricted to <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a>/<a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">EEA</a> nationals resident in the area. In the north (Lapland), it plays a major role in the local economy, while its economic impact is lesser in the southern parts of the area (<a href="/wiki/Province_of_Oulu" class="mw-redirect" title="Province of Oulu">Province of Oulu</a>).
</p><p>Among the reindeer herders in Sámi villages, the women usually have a higher level of formal education in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Games">Games</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Games">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Sámi have traditionally played both card games and board games, but few Sámi games have survived, in part because Christian missionaries and <a href="/wiki/Laestadianism" title="Laestadianism">Laestadianists</a> considered such games sinful.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Only the rules of three Sámi board games have been preserved into modern times. <i><a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1hkku" title="Sáhkku">Sáhkku</a></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Running-fight_game" title="Running-fight game">running-fight</a> <a href="/wiki/Board_games" class="mw-redirect" title="Board games">board game</a> where each player controls a set of soldiers (referred to as "women" and "men") that race across a board in a loop, attempting to eliminate the other player's soldiers. The game is related to South Scandinavian <a href="/wiki/Dald%C3%B8s" title="Daldøs">daldøs</a>, Arabian <a href="/wiki/T%C3%A2b" title="Tâb">tâb</a> and Indian tablan.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Sáhkku differs from these games in several respects, most notably the addition of a piece – "the king" – that changes gameplay radically. <a href="/wiki/Tafl_games#Tablut" title="Tafl games"><i>Tablut</i></a> is a pure <a href="/wiki/Strategy_game" title="Strategy game">strategy game</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Tafl_games" title="Tafl games">tafl</a> family. The game features "Swedes" and a "Swedish king" whose goal is to escape, and an army of "Muscovites" whose goal is to capture the king. Tablut is the only tafl game where a relatively intact set of rules have survived into our time. Hence, all modern versions of tafl (commonly called "Hnefatafl" and marketed exclusively as "Norse" or "Viking" games) are based on the Sámi game of tablut.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Dablot_Prejjesne" title="Dablot Prejjesne">Dablot Prejjesne</a></i> is a game related to <a href="/wiki/Alquerque" title="Alquerque">alquerque</a> which differs from most such games (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Draughts" title="Draughts">draughts</a>) by having pieces of three different ranks. The game's two sides are referred to as "Sámi" (king, prince, warriors) and "Finlenders" (landowners, landowner's son, farmers).<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_region">Cultural region</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Cultural region">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1pmi_(area)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sápmi (area)">Sápmi (area)</a></div>
<p>Sápmi is the name of the cultural region traditionally inhabited by the Sami people. Non-Sami and many regional maps have often called this same region <i>Lapland</i> as there is considerable regional overlap between the two terms. The overlap is, however, not complete:  <i>Lapland</i> covers only those parts of Sápmi that have fallen under Finnish jurisdiction, and most of the area having fallen under Swedish jurisdiction. The larger part of Sápmi is not covered by the term "Lapland". <i>Lapland</i> can be either misleading or offensive, or both, depending on the context and where this word is used, to the Sami.  Among the Sami people, <i>Sápmi</i> is strictly used and acceptable.
</p><p>Sápmi is located in Northern Europe, includes the northern parts of <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a> and spans four countries: Norway, Sweden, Finland, and Russia.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extent">Extent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Extent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_people_in_H%C3%A4rjedalen,_Sweden_(8539250322).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Sami_people_in_H%C3%A4rjedalen%2C_Sweden_%288539250322%29.jpg/220px-Sami_people_in_H%C3%A4rjedalen%2C_Sweden_%288539250322%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Sami_people_in_H%C3%A4rjedalen%2C_Sweden_%288539250322%29.jpg/330px-Sami_people_in_H%C3%A4rjedalen%2C_Sweden_%288539250322%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Sami_people_in_H%C3%A4rjedalen%2C_Sweden_%288539250322%29.jpg/440px-Sami_people_in_H%C3%A4rjedalen%2C_Sweden_%288539250322%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="867" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_people_in_H%C3%A4rjedalen,_Sweden_(8539250322).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sami people in <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A4rjedalen" title="Härjedalen">Härjedalen</a> (1790–1800), far south in the Sápmi area</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sapmi_Lappland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Sapmi_Lappland.jpg/220px-Sapmi_Lappland.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Sapmi_Lappland.jpg/330px-Sapmi_Lappland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Sapmi_Lappland.jpg/440px-Sapmi_Lappland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="354" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sapmi_Lappland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Laponian_area" title="Laponian area">Laponian area</a> in <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1pmi_(area)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sápmi (area)">Sápmi</a>, <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a></div></div></div>
<p>There is no official geographic definition for the boundaries of Sápmi. However, the following counties and provinces are usually included:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Dalarna_County" title="Dalarna County">Dalarna</a> county in Sweden</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Finnmark" title="Finnmark">Finnmark</a> county in Norway</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/J%C3%A4mtland_County" title="Jämtland County">Jämtland</a> county in Sweden</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lapland_(Finland)" title="Lapland (Finland)">Lapland region</a> in Finland</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Murmansk_Oblast" title="Murmansk Oblast">Murmansk oblast</a> in Russia</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nord-Tr%C3%B8ndelag" title="Nord-Trøndelag">Nord-Trøndelag</a> county in Norway</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nordland" title="Nordland">Nordland</a> county in Norway</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Norrbotten_County" title="Norrbotten County">Norrbotten</a> county in Sweden</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Troms" title="Troms">Troms</a> county in Norway</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/V%C3%A4sterbotten_County" title="Västerbotten County">Västerbotten</a> county in Sweden</li></ul>
<p>The municipalities of <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A4llivare" title="Gällivare">Gällivare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jokkmokk" title="Jokkmokk">Jokkmokk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arjeplog" title="Arjeplog">Arjeplog</a> in Swedish Lappland were designated a UNESCO <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a> in 1996 as a "Laponian Area".
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sami_Domicile_Area" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami Domicile Area">Sami Domicile Area</a> in Finland consists of the municipalities of <a href="/wiki/Enonteki%C3%B6" title="Enontekiö">Enontekiö</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utsjoki" title="Utsjoki">Utsjoki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inari,_Finland" title="Inari, Finland">Inari</a> as well as a part of the municipality of <a href="/wiki/Sodankyl%C3%A4" title="Sodankylä">Sodankylä</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Important_S.C3.A1mi_towns"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Important_Sámi_towns">Important Sámi towns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Important Sámi towns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9A%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B5.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/%D0%9A%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B5.jpg/220px-%D0%9A%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B5.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/%D0%9A%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B5.jpg/330px-%D0%9A%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/%D0%9A%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B5.jpg/440px-%D0%9A%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B5.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9A%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B5.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kanevka" title="Kanevka">Kanevka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ponoy_River" title="Ponoy River">Ponoy River</a>, Russia's <a href="/wiki/Lovozersky_District" title="Lovozersky District">Lovozersky District</a></div></div></div>
<p>The following towns and villages have a significant Sámi population or host Sámi institutions (Norwegian, Swedish, Finnish or Russian name in parentheses):
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Inari,_Finland" title="Inari, Finland">Aanaar, Anár, or Aanar</a> (Inari), is the location of the <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_Parliament_of_Finland" title="Sámi Parliament of Finland">Finnish Sámi Parliament</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sajos" title="Sajos">Sajos Sámi Cultural Centre</a>, SAKK –  <a href="/w/index.php?title=Saamelaisalueen_koulutuskeskus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Saamelaisalueen koulutuskeskus (page does not exist)">Saamelaisalueen koulutuskeskus</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://fi.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saamelaisalueen_koulutuskeskus" class="extiw" title="fi:Saamelaisalueen koulutuskeskus">fi</a>&#93;</span> (Sámi Education Institute), <a href="/wiki/Anar%C3%A2%C5%A1kiel%C3%A2_servi" title="Anarâškielâ servi">Anarâškielâ servi</a> (Inari Sámi Language Association), and the <a href="/wiki/Siida_(museum)" title="Siida (museum)">Inari Sámi Siida Museum</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hattfjelldal" title="Hattfjelldal">Aarborte</a> (Hattfjelldal) is a southern Sámi center with a Southern Sámi-language school and a Sámi culture center.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arjeplog" title="Arjeplog">Árjepluovve</a> (Arjeplog) is the Pite Saami center in Sweden.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Deatnu_Tana" class="mw-redirect" title="Deatnu Tana">Deatnu</a> (Tana) has a significant Sámi population.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tysfjord" title="Tysfjord">Divtasvuodna</a> (Tysfjord) is a center for the Lule-Sámi population. The <a href="/wiki/%C3%81rran" title="Árran">Árran</a> Lule-Sámi center is located here.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/G%C3%A1ivuotna" class="mw-redirect" title="Gáivuotna">Gáivuotna</a> (Kåfjord, Troms) is an important center for the Sea-Sámi culture. Each summer the <a href="/wiki/Riddu_Ri%C4%91%C4%91u" title="Riddu Riđđu">Riddu Riđđu</a> festival is held in Gáivuotna. The municipality has a Sámi-language center and hosts the <a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%81ja_S%C3%A1mi_Center&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ája Sámi Center (page does not exist)">Ája Sámi Center</a>. The opposition against Sámi language and culture revitalization in Gáivuotna was infamous in the late 1990s and included Sámi-language road signs being shot to pieces repeatedly.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kiruna" title="Kiruna">Giron</a> (Kiruna), proposed seat of the Swedish Sámi Parliament.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kautokeino_(village)" title="Kautokeino (village)">Guovdageaidnu</a> (Kautokeino) is perhaps the cultural capital of the Sámi. About 90% of the population speaks Sámi. Several Sámi institutions are located in Kautokeino including: <a href="/wiki/Beaivv%C3%A1%C5%A1_S%C3%A1mi_Theatre" class="mw-redirect" title="Beaivváš Sámi Theatre">Beaivváš Sámi Theatre</a>, a Sámi secondary school and reindeer-herding School, the <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_University_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Sámi University College">Sámi University College</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Sami_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Nordic Sami Institute">Nordic Sámi Research Institute</a>, the Sámi Language Board, the Resource Centre for the Rights of Indigenous People, and the International Centre for Reindeer Husbandry. In addition, several Sámi media are located in Kautokeino including the Sámi-language <i><a href="/wiki/%C3%81%C5%A1%C5%A1u" title="Áššu">Áššu</a></i> newspaper, and the DAT Sámi publishing house and record company. Kautokeino also hosts the , which includes the Sámi Grand Prix 2010 (Sámi Musicfestival) and the Reindeer Racing World Cup. The <a href="/wiki/Kautokeino_rebellion" title="Kautokeino rebellion">Kautokeino rebellion</a> in 1852 is one of the few Sámi rebellions against the Norwegian government's oppression against the Sámi.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Enonteki%C3%B6" title="Enontekiö">Iänudâh, or Eanodat</a> (Enontekiö).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/G%C3%A4llivare" title="Gällivare">Jiellevárri, or Váhčir</a> (Gällivare)</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Attje_Museum_Jokkmokk-exhibition.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Attje_Museum_Jokkmokk-exhibition.jpg/220px-Attje_Museum_Jokkmokk-exhibition.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Attje_Museum_Jokkmokk-exhibition.jpg/330px-Attje_Museum_Jokkmokk-exhibition.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Attje_Museum_Jokkmokk-exhibition.jpg/440px-Attje_Museum_Jokkmokk-exhibition.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1704" data-file-height="2272" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Attje_Museum_Jokkmokk-exhibition.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ájtte Museum of the Sámi people, Jokkmokk</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Utsjoki_Mantojarvi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Utsjoki_Mantojarvi.jpg/220px-Utsjoki_Mantojarvi.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Utsjoki_Mantojarvi.jpg/330px-Utsjoki_Mantojarvi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Utsjoki_Mantojarvi.jpg/440px-Utsjoki_Mantojarvi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2340" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Utsjoki_Mantojarvi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Log cabin in Utsjoki</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Jokkmokk" title="Jokkmokk">Jåhkåmåhkke</a> (Jokkmokk) holds a Sámi market on the first weekend of every February and has a Sámi school for language and traditional knowledge called <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.samernas.se/nord/">Samij Åhpadusguovdásj</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Karasjok_(village)" title="Karasjok (village)">Kárášjohka</a> (Karasjok) is the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Sami_Parliaments" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami Parliaments">Norwegian Sámi Parliament</a>. Other important Sámi institutions are located in Kárášjohka, including <a href="/wiki/NRK_S%C3%A1mi_Radio" class="mw-redirect" title="NRK Sámi Radio">NRK Sámi Radio</a>, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_Collections&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sámi Collections (page does not exist)">Sámi Collections</a> museum, the Sámi Art Centre, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_Specialist_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sámi Specialist Library (page does not exist)">Sámi Specialist Library</a>, the Mid-Finnmark legal office, a child and adolescent psychiatry outpatient clinic – one of few on a national level approved for providing full specialist training. Other significant institutions include a Sámi Specialist Medical Centre, and the Sámi Health Research Institute.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> In addition, the Sápmi cultural park is in the township, and the Sámi-language <i><a href="/wiki/Min_%C3%81igi" title="Min Áigi">Min Áigi</a></i> newspaper is published here.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lakselv" title="Lakselv">Leavdnja</a> (Lakselv) in <a href="/wiki/Porsanger" title="Porsanger">Porsáŋgu</a> (Porsanger) municipality is the location of the <a href="/wiki/Finnmark_Estate" class="mw-redirect" title="Finnmark Estate">Finnmark Estate</a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1gat" title="Ságat">Ságat</a></i> Sámi newspaper. The Finnmarkseiendommen organization owns and manages about 95% of the land in Finnmark, and 50% of its board members are elected by the Norwegian Sámi Parliament.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lovozero_(rural_locality)" title="Lovozero (rural locality)">Луя̄ввьр</a> (Lovozero)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/%C3%96stersund" title="Östersund">Staare</a> (Östersund) is the center for the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Sami_language" title="Southern Sami language">Southern Sámi</a> people living in Sweden. It is the site for <a href="/w/index.php?title=Gaaltije&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Gaaltije (page does not exist)">Gaaltije</a> – centre for South Sámi culture – a living source of knowledge for South Sámi culture, history and business. Staare also hosts the <a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_Information_Centre&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sámi Information Centre (page does not exist)">Sámi Information Centre</a> and one of the offices to the <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Sámi Parliament">Sámi Parliament</a> in Sweden.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Neiden,_Norway" title="Neiden, Norway">Njauddâm</a> is the center for the Skolt Sámi of Norway, which have their own museum <a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%84%CA%B9vv&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Äʹvv (page does not exist)">Äʹvv</a> in the town.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Utsjoki" title="Utsjoki">Ohcejohka</a> (Utsjoki).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sn%C3%A5sa" title="Snåsa">Snåase</a> (Snåsa) is a center for the Southern Sami language and the only municipality in Norway where Southern Sámi is an official language. The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Saemien_Sijte&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Saemien Sijte (page does not exist)">Saemien Sijte</a> Southern Sámi museum is located in Snåase.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Unj%C3%A1rga_Nesseby" class="mw-redirect" title="Unjárga Nesseby">Unjárga</a> (Nesseby) is an important center for the Sea Sámi culture. It is also the site for the <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A1rjjat_S%C3%A1mi_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Várjjat Sámi Museum">Várjjat Sámi Museum</a> and the Norwegian Sámi Parliament's department of culture and environment. The first Sámi to be elected into the <a href="/wiki/Storting" title="Storting">Norwegian Parliament</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isak_Saba" title="Isak Saba">Isak Saba</a>, was born there.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arvidsjaur" title="Arvidsjaur">Árviesjávrrie</a> (Arvidsjaur). New settlers from the south of Sweden didn't arrive until the second half of the 18th century. Because of that, Sámi tradition and culture has been well preserved. Sámi people living in the south of Norrbotten, Sweden, use the city for Reindeer herding during the summer. During winter they move the Reindeers to the coast, to Piteå.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_child.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Sami_child.jpg/220px-Sami_child.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="301" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Sami_child.jpg/330px-Sami_child.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Sami_child.jpg/440px-Sami_child.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1574" data-file-height="2153" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_child.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sámi child, 1923</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_family-easter.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sami_family-easter.jpg/220px-Sami_family-easter.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="295" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sami_family-easter.jpg/330px-Sami_family-easter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sami_family-easter.jpg/440px-Sami_family-easter.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1665" data-file-height="2232" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_family-easter.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sámi Specialist Library family at spring celebration</div></div></div>
<p>In the geographical area of Sápmi, the  Sámi Specialist Library are a small population. According to some, the estimated total Sámi population is about 70,000.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> One problem when attempting to count the population of the Sámi is that there are few common criteria of what "being a Sámi" constitutes. In addition, there are several Sámi languages and additional dialects, and there are several areas in <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1pmi_(area)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sápmi (area)">Sapmi</a> where few of the Sámi speak their <a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami languages">native language</a> due to the forced cultural assimilation, but still consider themselves Sami. Other identity markers are <a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">kinship</a> (which can be said to, at some level or other, be of high importance for all Sámi), the geographical region of Sápmi where their family came from, and/or protecting or preserving certain aspects of <a href="/wiki/Sami_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami culture">Sami culture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>All the Nordic Sámi Parliaments have included as the "core" criterion for registering as a Sámi the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_identity" title="Cultural identity">identity</a> in itself—one must declare that one truly considers oneself a Sámi. Objective criteria vary, but are generally related to kinship and/or language.
</p><p>Still, due to the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">cultural assimilation</a> of the Sámi people that had occurred in the four countries over the centuries, population estimates are difficult to measure precisely.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The population has been estimated to be between 80,000 and 135,000<sup id="cite_ref-cia.gov_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-norway.org.uk_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norway.org.uk-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> across the whole Nordic region, including urban areas such as <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">Oslo</a>, Norway, traditionally considered outside Sápmi. The Norwegian state recognizes any Norwegian as Sámi if he or she has one great-grandparent whose home language was Sámi, but there is not, and has never been, any registration of the home language spoken by Norwegian people.
</p><p>Roughly half of all Sámi live in Norway, but many live in Sweden, with smaller groups living in the far north of Finland and the <a href="/wiki/Kola_Peninsula" title="Kola Peninsula">Kola Peninsula</a> of Russia. The Sámi in Russia were forced by the Soviet authorities to relocate to a collective called <a href="/wiki/Lovozero_(rural_locality)" title="Lovozero (rural locality)">Lovozero</a>/Lujávri, in the central part of the Kola Peninsula.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_languages" title="Sámi languages">Sámi languages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:E.W._Borg_alphabet_1859.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/E.W._Borg_alphabet_1859.jpg/220px-E.W._Borg_alphabet_1859.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="345" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/E.W._Borg_alphabet_1859.jpg/330px-E.W._Borg_alphabet_1859.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/E.W._Borg_alphabet_1859.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="627" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:E.W._Borg_alphabet_1859.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>E.W. Borg alphabet book, published in 1859 in Finnish-<a href="/wiki/Inari_Sami_language" title="Inari Sami language">Inari Sámi</a></div></div></div>
<p>There is no single Sámi language, but a group of ten distinct <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_languages" title="Sámi languages">Sámi languages</a>. Six of these languages have their own written standards. The Sámi languages are relatively closely related, but not mutually intelligible; for instance, speakers of Southern Sámi cannot understand Northern Sámi. Especially earlier, these distinct languages were referred to as "dialects", but today, this is considered misleading due to the deep differences between the varieties. Most Sámi languages are spoken in several countries, because linguistic borders do not correspond to national borders.
</p><p>All Sámi languages are at some degree of endangerment, ranging from what <a href="/wiki/Unesco" class="mw-redirect" title="Unesco">UNESCO</a> defines as "definitely endangered" to "extinct".<sup id="cite_ref-F._Moseley_2010_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-F._Moseley_2010-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> This is due in part to historic laws prohibiting the use of Sámi languages in schools and at home in Sweden and Norway. Sámi languages, and Sámi song-chants, called yoiks, were illegal in Norway from 1773 until 1958. Then, access to Sámi instruction as part of schooling was not available until 1988. Special residential schools that would assimilate the Sámi into the dominant culture were established. These were originally run by missionaries, but later, controlled by the government. For example, in Russia, Sámi children were taken away when aged 1–2 and returned when aged 15–17 with no knowledge of their language and traditional communities. Not all Sámi viewed the schools negatively, and not all of the schools were brutal. However, being taken from home and prohibited from speaking Sámi has resulted in cultural alienation, loss of language, and lowered self-esteem.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sámi languages belong to the <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic</a> language family, linguistically related to <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a>. Due to prolonged contact and import of items foreign to Sámi culture from neighboring Scandinavians, there are a number of <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> loanwords in Sámi, particularly for "urban" objects. The majority of the Sámi now speak the majority languages of the countries they live in, i.e., Swedish, Russian, Finnish and Norwegian. Efforts are being made to further the use of Sámi languages among Sámi and persons of Sámi origin. Despite these changes, the legacy of cultural repression still exists.  Many older Sámi still refuse to speak Sámi.  In addition, Sámi parents still feel alienated from schools and hence do not participate as much as they could in shaping school curricula and policy.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Norway, the name of the language is <i>samisk</i>, and the name of the people is <i>Same</i>; in Finland, the name of the language is spelled <i>saame</i> and the name of the people <i>saamelainen</i>.
</p><p>American scientist <a href="/wiki/Michael_E._Krauss" title="Michael E. Krauss">Michael E. Krauss</a> published in 1997 an estimate of Sámi population and their languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Krauss97_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krauss97-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
