<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Corrected_sami_map_4.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Corrected_sami_map_4.PNG/220px-Corrected_sami_map_4.PNG" decoding="async" width="220" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Corrected_sami_map_4.PNG/330px-Corrected_sami_map_4.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Corrected_sami_map_4.PNG/440px-Corrected_sami_map_4.PNG 2x" data-file-width="1107" data-file-height="1329" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Corrected_sami_map_4.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Geographic distribution of the Sami languages: <div><ol><li>Southern Sami</li><li>Ume Sami</li><li>Pite Sami</li><li>Lule Sami</li><li>Northern Sami</li><li>Skolt Sami</li><li>Inari Sami</li><li>Kildin Sami</li><li>Ter Sami</li></ol></div> Darkened area represents municipalities that recognize Sámi as an official language.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kemi_Sami_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Kemi Sami language">Kemi Sami language</a> became extinct in the 19th century.
</p><p>Many Sámi do not speak any of the Sámi languages any more due to historical assimilation policies, so the number of Sámi living in each area is much higher.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Intelligence" title="Intelligence">Intelligence</a> studies of Sami have found them to score similarly to other Nordic populations.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Similar to Asian peoples, they have a relative strength in visuospatial ability and weakness in verbal ability.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Division_by_geography">Division by geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Division by geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sápmi is traditionally divided into:
</p>
<ul><li>Eastern Sapmi (Inari, Skolt, Akkala, Kildin and Teri Sámi in Kola peninsula (Russia) and Inari (Finland, formerly also in eastern Norway)</li>
<li>Northern Sápmi (Northern, Lule and Pite Sámi in most of northern parts of Norway, Sweden and Finland)</li>
<li>Southern Sápmi (Ume and Southern Sámi in central parts of Sweden and Norway)</li></ul>
<p>It should also be noted that many Sámi now live outside Sápmi, in large cities such as <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">Oslo</a> in Norway.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Division_by_occupation">Division by occupation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Division by occupation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A division often used in Northern Sámi is based on occupation and the area of living. This division is also used in many historical texts:<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Reindeer Sámi or Mountain Sámi (in Northern Sámi boazosapmelash or badjeolmmosh). Previously nomadic Sámi living as reindeer herders. Now most have a permanent residence in the Sámi core areas. Some 10% of Sámi practice reindeer herding, which is seen as a fundamental part of a Sámi culture and, in some parts of the Nordic countries, can be practiced by Sámis only.</li>
<li>Sea Sámi (in Northern Sámi" <i>mearasapmelash</i>). These lived traditionally by combining fishing and small-scale farming. Today, often used for all Sámi from the coast regardless of their occupation.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Skogssamer" class="mw-redirect" title="Skogssamer">Forest Sámi</a> who traditionally lived by combining fishing in inland rivers and lakes with small-scale reindeer-herding.</li>
<li>City Sámi who are now probably the largest group of Sámi.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Division_by_country">Division by country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Division by country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:S%C3%A1mi_presentation_in_the_cultural_Centre_in_Lovozero,_Kola_Peninsula,_Russia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/S%C3%A1mi_presentation_in_the_cultural_Centre_in_Lovozero%2C_Kola_Peninsula%2C_Russia.jpg/220px-S%C3%A1mi_presentation_in_the_cultural_Centre_in_Lovozero%2C_Kola_Peninsula%2C_Russia.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/S%C3%A1mi_presentation_in_the_cultural_Centre_in_Lovozero%2C_Kola_Peninsula%2C_Russia.jpg/330px-S%C3%A1mi_presentation_in_the_cultural_Centre_in_Lovozero%2C_Kola_Peninsula%2C_Russia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/S%C3%A1mi_presentation_in_the_cultural_Centre_in_Lovozero%2C_Kola_Peninsula%2C_Russia.jpg/440px-S%C3%A1mi_presentation_in_the_cultural_Centre_in_Lovozero%2C_Kola_Peninsula%2C_Russia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2467" data-file-height="2836" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:S%C3%A1mi_presentation_in_the_cultural_Centre_in_Lovozero,_Kola_Peninsula,_Russia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sámi traditional presentation in Lovozero, <a href="/wiki/Kola_Peninsula" title="Kola Peninsula">Kola Peninsula</a>, Russia</div></div></div>
<p>According to the Norwegian Sámi Parliament, the Sámi population of Norway is 40,000. If all people who speak Sámi or have a parent, grandparent, or great-grandparent who speaks or spoke Sámi are included, the number reaches 70,000. As of 2005, 12,538 people were registered to vote in the election for the Sámi Parliament in Norway.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Needs valid source (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The bulk of the Sámi live in Finnmark and Northern <a href="/wiki/Troms" title="Troms">Troms</a>, but there are also Sámi populations in Southern Troms, <a href="/wiki/Nordland" title="Nordland">Nordland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tr%C3%B8ndelag" title="Trøndelag">Trøndelag</a>. Due to recent migration, it has also been claimed that <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">Oslo</a> is the municipality with the largest Sámi population. The Sámi are in a majority only in the municipalities of <a href="/wiki/Kautokeino" title="Kautokeino">Guovdageaidnu-Kautokeino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karasjok" title="Karasjok">Karasjohka-Karasjok</a>, <a href="/wiki/Porsanger" title="Porsanger">Porsanger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tana,_Norway" title="Tana, Norway">Deatnu-Tana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nesseby" title="Nesseby">Unjargga-Nesseby</a> in Finnmark, and <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A1ivuotna" class="mw-redirect" title="Gáivuotna">Gáivuotna</a> (Kåfjord) in Northern Troms. This area is also known as the Sámi core area. Sámi and Norwegian are equal as administrative languages in this area.
</p><p>In Norway, Sweden and Finland Sámi are primarily Lutheran; Skolt Sámi of Finland and Sámi of Russia are primarily orthodox Christians.
</p><p>According to the Swedish Sámi Parliament, the Sámi population of Sweden is about 20,000.
</p><p>According to the Finnish Population Registry Center and the Finnish Sámi Parliament, the Sámi population living in Finland was 7,371 in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> As of 31 December 2006, only 1776 of them had registered to speak one of the Sami languages as the mother tongue.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the 2002 census, the Sámi population of Russia was 1,991.
</p><p>Since 1926, the number of identified Sámi in Russia has gradually increased:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Census 1926</i>: 1,720 (this number refers to the entire Soviet Union)</li>
<li><i>Census 1939</i>: 1,829</li>
<li><i>Census 1959</i>: 1,760</li>
<li><i>Census 1970</i>: 1,836</li>
<li><i>Census 1979</i>: 1,775</li>
<li><i>Census 1989</i>: 1,835</li>
<li><i>Census 2002</i>: 1,991</li></ul>
<h3><span id="S.C3.A1mi_immigration_outside_of_Sapmi"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sámi_immigration_outside_of_Sapmi">Sámi immigration outside of Sapmi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Sámi immigration outside of Sapmi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RoundupNunivak1964FWS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/RoundupNunivak1964FWS.jpg/220px-RoundupNunivak1964FWS.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/RoundupNunivak1964FWS.jpg/330px-RoundupNunivak1964FWS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/RoundupNunivak1964FWS.jpg/440px-RoundupNunivak1964FWS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1050" data-file-height="837" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RoundupNunivak1964FWS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reindeer in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a></div></div></div>
<p>There are an estimated 30,000 people living in North America who are either Sámi, or descendants of Sámi.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>  Most have settled in areas that are known to have Norwegian, Swedish and Finnish immigrants. Some of these concentrated areas are <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Peninsula_of_Michigan" title="Upper Peninsula of Michigan">Upper Peninsula of Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a> and Alaska; and throughout Canada, including <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ontario" title="Northern Ontario">Northern Ontario</a>, and the Canadian territories of the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territories" title="Northwest Territories">Northwest Territories</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yukon" title="Yukon">Yukon</a>, and the territory now known as <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>.
</p><p>Descendants of these Sámi immigrants typically know little of their heritage because their ancestors purposely hid their indigenous culture to avoid discrimination from the dominating Scandinavian or Nordic culture. Though some of these Sámi are diaspora that moved to North America in order to escape assimilation policies in their home countries, many continued to downplay their Sámi culture in an internalization of colonial viewpoints about indigenous peoples and in order for them to try to blend into their respective Nordic cultures.  There were also several Sámi families that were brought to North America with herds of reindeer by the U.S. and Canadian governments as part of the "Reindeer Project" designed to teach the Inuit about reindeer herding.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> There is a long history of <a href="/wiki/Sami_in_Alaska" title="Sami in Alaska">Sami in Alaska</a>.
</p><p>Some of these Sámi immigrants and descendants of immigrants are members of the <a href="/wiki/Sami_Siida_of_North_America" title="Sami Siida of North America">Sami Siida of North America</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization">Organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sápmi demonstrates a distinct semi-national identity that transcends the borders between Norway, Sweden, Finland and Russia. There is no movement for sovereign state, but they do seek greater autonomy in respective nation states.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sami_Parliaments">Sami Parliaments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Sami Parliaments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sami_Parliaments" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami Parliaments">Sami Parliaments</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:3NorwSamiPresidents.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/3NorwSamiPresidents.jpg/220px-3NorwSamiPresidents.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/3NorwSamiPresidents.jpg/330px-3NorwSamiPresidents.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/3NorwSamiPresidents.jpg/440px-3NorwSamiPresidents.jpg 2x" data-file-width="669" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:3NorwSamiPresidents.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sven-Roald_Nyst%C3%B8" title="Sven-Roald Nystø">Sven-Roald Nystø</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aili_Keskitalo" title="Aili Keskitalo">Aili Keskitalo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ole_Henrik_Magga" title="Ole Henrik Magga">Ole Henrik Magga</a>, the three first presidents of the <a href="/wiki/Sami_Parliament_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami Parliament of Norway">Sami Parliament of Norway</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Sami Parliaments (<i>Sámediggi</i> in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Sami" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Sami">Northern Sami</a>, <i>Sämitigge</i> in <a href="/wiki/Inari_Sami_language" title="Inari Sami language">Inari Sami</a>, <i>Sää'mte'ǧǧ</i> in <a href="/wiki/Skolt_Sami_language" title="Skolt Sami language">Skolt Sami</a>) founded in Finland (1973), Norway (1989) and Sweden (1993) are the representative bodies for peoples of Sami heritage. Russia has not recognized the Sami as a minority and, as a result, recognizes no Sami parliament, even if the Sami people there have formed an unrecognised <a href="/wiki/Sami_Parliament_of_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami Parliament of Russia">Sami Parliament of Russia</a>.  There is no single, unified Sami parliament that spans across the Nordic countries. Rather, each of the aforementioned three countries has set up its own separate legislatures for Sami people, even though the three Sami Parliaments often work together on cross-border issues.  In all three countries, they act as an institution of cultural autonomy for the indigenous Sami people. The parliaments have very weak political influence, far from autonomy. They are formally public authorities, ruled by the Scandinavian governments, but have democratically elected parliamentarians, whose mission is to work for the Sami people and culture. Candidates' election promises often get into conflict with the institutions' submission under their governments, but as authorities, they have some influence over the government.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Norwegian_organizations">Norwegian organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Norwegian organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The main organisations for Sami representation in Norway are the <i>siidas</i>. They cover northern and central Norway.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Swedish_organizations">Swedish organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Swedish organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The main organisations for Sami representation in Sweden are the <i>siidas</i>. They cover northern and central Sweden.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finnish_organizations">Finnish organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Finnish organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In contrast to Norway and Sweden, in Finland, a <i>siida</i> (<i>paliskunta</i> in Finnish) is a reindeer-herding corporation that is not restricted by ethnicity. There are indeed some ethnic Finns who practice reindeer herding, and in principle, all residents of the reindeer herding area (most of Finnish Lapland and parts of Oulu province) who are citizens of <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">EEA</a> countries,<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> i.e., the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> and Norway, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>, are allowed to join a <i>paliskunta</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russian_organizations">Russian organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Russian organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2010, the Sami Council supported the establishment of a cultural center in Russia for Arctic peoples. The Center for Northern Peoples aims to promote artistic and cultural cooperation between the Arctic peoples of Russia and the Nordic countries, with particular focus on indigenous peoples and minorities.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Border_conflicts">Border conflicts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Border conflicts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glen1_Sweden.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Glen1_Sweden.jpg/220px-Glen1_Sweden.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Glen1_Sweden.jpg/330px-Glen1_Sweden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Glen1_Sweden.jpg/440px-Glen1_Sweden.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glen1_Sweden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Land rights for grazing <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a></div></div></div>
<p>Sápmi, the Sami traditional lands, cross four national borders. Traditional summer and winter pastures sometimes lie on different sides of the borders of the nation-states. In addition to that, there is a border drawn for modern-day <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1pmi_(area)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sápmi (area)">Sápmi</a>. Some state that the rights (for reindeer herding and, in some parts, even for fishing and hunting) include not only modern Sápmi but areas that are beyond today's Sápmi that reflect older territories. Today's "borders" originate from the 14th to 16th centuries when land-owning conflicts occurred. The establishment of more stable dwelling places and larger towns originates from the 16th century and was performed for strategic defence and economic reasons, both by peoples from Sami groups themselves and more southern immigrants.
</p><p>Owning land within the borders or being a member of a <i><a href="/wiki/Siida" title="Siida">siida</a></i> (Sami corporation) gives rights. A different law enacted in Sweden in the mid-1990s gave the right to anyone to fish and hunt in the region, something that was met with skepticism and anger amongst the <i>siidas</i>.
</p><p>Court proceedings have been common throughout history, and the aim from the Sami viewpoint is to reclaim territories used earlier in history. Due to a major defeat in 1996, one <i>siida</i> has introduced a sponsorship "Reindeer Godfather" concept to raise funds for further battles in courts. These "internal conflicts" are usually conflicts between non-Sami land owners and reindeer owners. Cases question the Sami ancient rights to reindeer pastures. In 2010, Sweden was criticized for its relations with the Sami in the Universal Periodic Review conducted by the Working Group of the Human Rights Council.<sup id="cite_ref-lib.ohchr.org_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lib.ohchr.org-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The question whether the <a href="/wiki/Fjeld" class="mw-redirect" title="Fjeld">fjeld</a>'s territory is owned by the governments (crown land) or by the Sami population is not answered.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>From an indigenous perspective, people "belong to the land", the land does not belong to people, but this does not mean that hunters, herders, and fishing people do not know where the borders of their territories are located as well as those of their neighbors.<sup id="cite_ref-Osherenko_80-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osherenko-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="National_symbols">National symbols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: National symbols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although the Sami have considered themselves to be one people throughout history, the idea of Sápmi, a Sami <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nation</a>, first gained acceptance among the Sami in the 1970s, and even later among the majority population. During the 1980s and 1990s, a flag was created, a national song was written, and the date of a national day was settled.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flag">Flag</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Flag">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sami_flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami flag">Sami flag</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_flag.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Sami_flag.svg/220px-Sami_flag.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Sami_flag.svg/330px-Sami_flag.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Sami_flag.svg/440px-Sami_flag.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2020" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sami_flag.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sami flag</div></div></div>
<p>The Sami flag was inaugurated during the Sami Conference in <a href="/wiki/%C3%85re" title="Åre">Åre</a>, Sweden, on 15 August 1986. It was the result of a competition for which many suggestions were entered. The winning design was submitted by the artist <a href="/wiki/Astrid_B%C3%A5hl" title="Astrid Båhl">Astrid Båhl</a> from <a href="/wiki/Skibotn" title="Skibotn">Skibotn</a>, Norway.
</p><p>The motif (shown right) was derived from the shaman's drum and the poem "Paiven parneh" ("Sons of the Sun") by the South Sami <a href="/w/index.php?title=Anders_Fjellner&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Anders Fjellner (page does not exist)">Anders Fjellner</a> describing the Sami as sons and daughters of the sun. The flag has the Sami colours, red, green, yellow and blue, and the circle represents the sun (red) and the moon (blue).
</p>
<h3><span id="The_Sami_People.27s_Day"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Sami_People's_Day">The Sami People's Day</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: The Sami People&#039;s Day">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sami_National_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami National Day">Sami National Day</a></div>
<p>The Sami National Day falls on February 6 as this date was when the <a href="/wiki/The_Sami_assembly_of_1917" class="mw-redirect" title="The Sami assembly of 1917">first Sami congress</a> was held in 1917 in <a href="/wiki/Trondheim" title="Trondheim">Trondheim</a>, Norway. This congress was the first time that Norwegian and Swedish Sami came together across their national borders to work together to find solutions for common problems. The resolution for celebrating on 6 February was passed in 1992 at the 15th Sami congress in Helsinki. Since 1993, Norway, Sweden and Finland have recognized February 6 as Sami National Day.
</p>
<h3><span id=".22Song_of_the_Sami_People.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Song_of_the_Sami_People&quot;">"Song of the Sami People"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: &quot;Song of the Sami People&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_soga_l%C3%A1vlla" class="mw-redirect" title="Sámi soga lávlla">Sámi soga lávlla</a></div>
<p>"Sámi soga lávlla" ("Song of the Sami People", lit. "Song of the Sami Family") was originally a poem written by <a href="/wiki/Isak_Saba" title="Isak Saba">Isak Saba</a> that was published in the newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Sagai_Muittal%C3%A6gje" class="mw-redirect" title="Sagai Muittalægje">Sagai Muittalægje</a></i> for the first time on 1 April 1906. In August 1986, it became the <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">national anthem</a> of the Sami. Arne Sørli set the poem to music, which was then approved at the 15th Sami Conference in <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a> in 1992. "Sámi soga lávlla" has been translated into all of the <a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami languages">Sami languages</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coats_of_Arms_of_Sami_Communities">Coats of Arms of Sami Communities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Coats of Arms of Sami Communities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sami_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami religion">Sami religion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shaman.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Shaman.jpg/220px-Shaman.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Shaman.jpg/330px-Shaman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Shaman.jpg/440px-Shaman.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1313" data-file-height="1792" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shaman.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper etching (1767) by O.H. von Lode showing a <a href="/wiki/Noaidi" title="Noaidi">noaidi</a> with his meavrresgárri <a href="/wiki/Drum" title="Drum">drum</a></div></div></div>
<p>Widespread <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">Shamanism</a> persisted among the Sami up until the 18th century. Most Sami today belong to the state-run <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> churches of Norway, Sweden and Finland. Some Sami in Russia belong to the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a>, and similarly, some Skolt Sami resettled in Finland are also part of an <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a> congregation, with an additional small population in Norway.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_Sami_religion">Traditional Sami religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Traditional Sami religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Traditional Sami religion was a type of <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheism</a>. (See <a href="/wiki/Sami_shamanism#Deities" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami shamanism">Sami deities</a>.) There was some diversity due to the wide area that is <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1pmi_(area)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sápmi (area)">Sápmi</a>, allowing for the evolution of variations in beliefs and practices between tribes. The old beliefs are closely connected to the land, <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animism</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>. <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_shamanism" title="Sámi shamanism">Sami spirituality</a> is often characterized by <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheism</a>, a strong emphasis on the importance of personal spirituality and its interconnectivity with one's own daily life, and a deep connection between the natural and spiritual "worlds".<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Among other roles, the <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_shamanism" title="Sámi shamanism">Sami Shaman</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Noaidi" title="Noaidi">noaidi</a>, enabled ritual communication with the supernatural<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> through the use of tools such as drums, chants, sacred objects and <a href="/wiki/Amanita_muscaria" title="Amanita muscaria">fly agaric</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gusto_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gusto-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Some practices within the Old Sami religion included natural sacred sites such as mountains, springs, land formations, as well as man-made ones such as <a href="/wiki/Petroglyph" title="Petroglyph">petroglyphs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Labyrinth" title="Labyrinth">labyrinths</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sami cosmology divides the universe into three worlds. The upper world is related to the South, warmth, life, and the color white. It is also the dwelling of the gods. The middle world is like the Norse <a href="/wiki/Midgard" title="Midgard">Midgard</a>, it is the dwelling of humans and it is associated with the color red. The third world is the underworld and it is associated with the color black, it represents the north, the cold and it is inhabited by otters, loons, and seals and mythical animals.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sami religion shared some elements with <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse mythology</a>, possibly from early contacts with trading Vikings (or vice versa). Through a mainly French initiative from <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Paul_Gaimard" title="Joseph Paul Gaimard">Joseph Paul Gaimard</a> as part of his <a href="/wiki/La_Recherche_Expedition_(1838%E2%80%931840)" class="mw-redirect" title="La Recherche Expedition (1838–1840)">La Recherche Expedition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lars_Levi_L%C3%A6stadius" class="mw-redirect" title="Lars Levi Læstadius">Lars Levi Læstadius</a> began research on Sami mythology. His work resulted in <a href="/wiki/Fragments_of_Lappish_Mythology" title="Fragments of Lappish Mythology">Fragments of Lappish Mythology</a>, since by his own admission, they contained only a small percentage of what had existed. The fragments were termed <i>Theory of Gods</i>, <i>Theory of Sacrifice</i>, <i>Theory of Prophecy, or short reports about rumorous Sami magic</i> and <i>Sami sagas</i>. Generally, he claims to have filtered out the Norse influence and derived common elements between the South, North, and Eastern Sami groups. The mythology has common elements with other traditional indigenous religions as well—such as those in <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> and North America.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Missionary_efforts">Missionary efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Missionary efforts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tore_Johnsen,_samiske_kirkedager.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Tore_Johnsen%2C_samiske_kirkedager.JPG/220px-Tore_Johnsen%2C_samiske_kirkedager.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Tore_Johnsen%2C_samiske_kirkedager.JPG/330px-Tore_Johnsen%2C_samiske_kirkedager.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/Tore_Johnsen%2C_samiske_kirkedager.JPG 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="533" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tore_Johnsen,_samiske_kirkedager.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sermon at the 2004 <i>Samiske kirkedager</i></div></div></div>
<p>The term <i>Sami religion</i> usually refers to the traditional religion, practiced by most Sámi until approximately the 18th century. <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic</a> missionaries as early as the 13th century. Increased pressure came after the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rune_drum" class="mw-redirect" title="Rune drum">rune drums</a> were burned or sent to museums abroad. In this period, many Sami practiced their traditional religion at home, while going to church on Sunday. Since the Sami were considered to possess "witchcraft" powers, they were often accused of sorcery during the 17th century and were the subjects of witchcraft trials and burnings.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Norway, a major effort to convert the Sami was made around 1720, when <a href="/wiki/Thomas_von_Westen" title="Thomas von Westen">Thomas von Westen</a>, the "Apostle of the Sami", burned drums, burned sacred objects, and converted people.<sup id="cite_ref-Holloway_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holloway-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Out of the estimated thousands of drums prior to this period, only about 70 are known to remain today, scattered in museums around Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Gusto_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gusto-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Sacred sites were destroyed, such as sieidi (stones in natural or human-built formations), álda and sáivu (sacred hills), springs, caves and other natural formations where offerings were made.
</p><p>In the far east of the Sami area, the Russian monk Trifon converted the Sami in the 16th century. Today, St. George's chapel in <a href="/wiki/Neiden,_Finnmark" class="mw-redirect" title="Neiden, Finnmark">Neiden</a>, Norway (1565), testifies to this effort.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laestadius">Laestadius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Laestadius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Lars_Levi_Laestadius" title="Lars Levi Laestadius">Lars Levi Laestadius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laestadianism" title="Laestadianism">Laestadianism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rune_drum_S%C3%A1pmi_MEK.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Rune_drum_S%C3%A1pmi_MEK.jpg/170px-Rune_drum_S%C3%A1pmi_MEK.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Rune_drum_S%C3%A1pmi_MEK.jpg/255px-Rune_drum_S%C3%A1pmi_MEK.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Rune_drum_S%C3%A1pmi_MEK.jpg/340px-Rune_drum_S%C3%A1pmi_MEK.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2039" data-file-height="3160" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rune_drum_S%C3%A1pmi_MEK.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Noaidi" title="Noaidi">Noaidi</a> drum</div></div></div>
<p>Around 1840 Swedish Sami <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Sweden" title="Church of Sweden">Lutheran</a> pastor and administrator <a href="/wiki/Lars_Levi_Laestadius" title="Lars Levi Laestadius">Lars Levi Laestadius</a> initiated among the Sami a puritanical <a href="/wiki/Pietism" title="Pietism">pietist</a> movement emphasizing <a href="/wiki/Abstinence#Alcohol" title="Abstinence">complete abstinence from alcohol</a>. This movement is still very dominant in Sami-speaking areas. Laestadius spoke many languages, and he became fluent and preached in Finnish and <a href="/wiki/Northern_S%C3%A1mi_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Sámi language">Northern Sami</a> in addition to his native <a href="/wiki/Southern_S%C3%A1mi_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Sámi language">Southern Sami</a> and Swedish,<sup id="cite_ref-Trans._Börje_Vähämäki_2002_p.24_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trans._Börje_Vähämäki_2002_p.24-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> the language he used for scholarly publications.<sup id="cite_ref-Holloway_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holloway-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Two great challenges Laestadius had faced since his early days as a church minister were the indifference of his Sami parishioners, who had been forced by the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Sweden" title="Government of Sweden">Swedish government</a> to convert from their <a href="/wiki/Sami_shamanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami shamanism">shamanistic</a> religion to Lutheranism, and the misery caused them by alcoholism.  The spiritual understanding Laestadius acquired and shared in his new sermons "filled with vivid metaphors from the lives of the Sami that they could understand,&#160;... about a God who cared about the lives of the people" had a profound positive effect on both problems.  One account from a Sami cultural perspective recalls a new desire among the Sami to learn to read and a "bustle and energy in the church, with people confessing their sins, crying and praying for forgiveness&#160;... [Alcohol abuse] and the theft of [the Sa`mis'] reindeer diminished, which had a positive influence on the Sami's relationships, finances and family life."<sup id="cite_ref-Sapmi_-_Religion_-_Laestadianism_-_Nils_Gustav_Labba,_Editor_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sapmi_-_Religion_-_Laestadianism_-_Nils_Gustav_Labba,_Editor-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-shamanism_and_traditional_healing">Neo-shamanism and traditional healing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Neo-shamanism and traditional healing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Today there are a number of Sami who seek to return to the traditional <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">Pagan</a> values of their ancestors. There are also some Sami who claim to be <i>noaidi</i> and offer their services through newspaper advertisements, in <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> arrangements, or for tourist groups. While they practice a religion based on that of their ancestors, widespread anti-pagan <a href="/wiki/Discrimination_against_Neopagans" class="mw-redirect" title="Discrimination against Neopagans">prejudice</a> has caused these shamans to be generally not viewed as part of an unbroken Sami religious tradition.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Traditional Sámi beliefs are composed of three intertwining elements: animism, shamanism, and polytheism. Sámi animism is manifested in the Sámi's belief that all significant natural objects (such as animals, plants, rocks, etc.) possess a soul; and from a polytheistic perspective, traditional Sámi beliefs include a multitude of spirits.<sup id="cite_ref-Holloway_138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holloway-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Many contemporary practitioners are compared to practitioners of <a href="/wiki/Neo-paganism" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-paganism">neo-paganism</a>, as a number of neopagan religions likewise combine elements of ancient pagan religions with more recent revisions or innovations, but others feel they are attempting to revive or reconstruct indigenous Sami religions as found in historic, folkloric sources and oral traditions.
</p><p>In 2012, County Governor of Troms approved Shamanic Association of Tromsø as a new religion.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A very different religious idea is represented by the numerous "wise men" and "wise women" found throughout the Sami area. They often offer to heal the sick through rituals and traditional medicines and may also combine traditional elements, such as older Sami teachings, with newer monotheistic inventions that Christian missionaries taught their ancestors, such as readings from the Bible.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_studies">Genetic studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Genetic studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Population_genetics_of_the_Sami" class="mw-redirect" title="Population genetics of the Sami">Population genetics of the Sami</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lapland_Mother_NGM-v31-p556.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Lapland_Mother_NGM-v31-p556.jpg/220px-Lapland_Mother_NGM-v31-p556.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="314" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Lapland_Mother_NGM-v31-p556.jpg/330px-Lapland_Mother_NGM-v31-p556.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Lapland_Mother_NGM-v31-p556.jpg/440px-Lapland_Mother_NGM-v31-p556.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1551" data-file-height="2215" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lapland_Mother_NGM-v31-p556.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sami mother with her children</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Anthropologist" title="Anthropologist">Anthropologists</a> have been studying the Sami people for hundreds of years for their assumed physical and cultural differences from the rest of the Europeans. Recent <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic studies</a> have indicated that the two most frequent <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">maternal lineages</a> of the Sámi people are the haplogroups <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_V_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup V (mtDNA)">V</a> (neolithic in Europe and not found in Finland 1500 years ago<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>) and <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_U_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup U (mtDNA)">U5b</a> (ancient in Europe).  "The Y-chromosomal variety in the Saami is also consistent with their European ancestry. It suggests that the large genetic separation of the Saami from other Europeans is best explained by assuming that the Saami are descendants of a narrow, distinctive subset of Europeans."<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Y-chromosome haplogroup N-VL29 makes up 20%, came from Siberia 3500 years ago or more likely much later. Y-chromosome N-Z1936 makes up 20%, likely came from Siberia with Sami language later. This tallies with archeological evidence suggesting that several different cultural groups made their way to the core area of Sami from 8000 to 6000&#160;BC,<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> presumably including some of the ancestors of present-day Sami. The "<a href="/wiki/Nganasan_people" title="Nganasan people">Nganassan</a>" autosomic component now makes up more than 25% in the Sami, but was 50% in the 3500-year old Kola population.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The Mesolithic "Western European hunter-gatherer" (WHG) component is close to 15%, while that of the Neolithic "European early farmer" (LBK) is 10%. 50% is the Bronze Age "<a href="/wiki/Yamna_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Yamna culture">Yamna</a>" component, the earliest trace of which is observed in the <a href="/wiki/Pit%E2%80%93Comb_Ware_culture" title="Pit–Comb Ware culture">Pit–Comb Ware culture</a> in <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, but in a 2.5-fold lower percentage.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_scientific_research_carried_out_on_the_Sami">History of scientific research carried out on the Sami</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: History of scientific research carried out on the Sami">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Friedl%C3%A4nder.plakat.7.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Friedl%C3%A4nder.plakat.7.jpg/170px-Friedl%C3%A4nder.plakat.7.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Friedl%C3%A4nder.plakat.7.jpg/255px-Friedl%C3%A4nder.plakat.7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Friedl%C3%A4nder.plakat.7.jpg/340px-Friedl%C3%A4nder.plakat.7.jpg 2x" data-file-width="626" data-file-height="888" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Friedl%C3%A4nder.plakat.7.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ad for an 1893/1894 ethnological exposition of Sámi in <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>-Saint Paul</div></div></div>
<p>The genetic makeup of Sami people has been extensively studied for as long as such research has been in existence. Ethnographic photography of the Sami began with the invention of the camera in the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> This continued on into the 1920s and 1930s, when Sami were photographed naked and anatomically measured by scientists, with the help of the local police—sometimes at gunpoint—to collect data that would justify their own racial theories.<sup id="cite_ref-GiveUs_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GiveUs-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Thus, there is a degree of distrust by some in the Sami community towards genetic research.<sup id="cite_ref-GiveUs_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GiveUs-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Examples of discriminatory actions include the <a href="/wiki/Statens_institut_f%C3%B6r_rasbiologi" title="Statens institut för rasbiologi">Statens Institut for Rasbiologi</a> compulsory sterilization project for Sami women, which continued until 1975,<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> and Sami graves being plundered to provide research materials,<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> of which their remains and artifacts from this period from across Sápmi can still be found in various state collections.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>  In the late 19th century, colonial fascination with Arctic peoples led to human beings exhibited in <a href="/wiki/Human_zoos" class="mw-redirect" title="Human zoos">human zoos</a>.  Sami people were exhibited with their traditional <a href="/wiki/Lavvu" title="Lavvu">lavvu</a> tents, weapons, and sleds, beside a group of reindeer at <a href="/wiki/Tierpark_Hagenbeck" title="Tierpark Hagenbeck">Tierpark Hagenbeck</a><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> and other zoos across the globe.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_people_of_Sami_descent">Notable people of Sami descent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: Notable people of Sami descent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louise_B%C3%A4ckman&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Louise Bäckman (page does not exist)">Louise Bäckman</a>  (1926–present) Born in Tärnaby, Ume sami speaker. Professor emeritus. She has carried out several studies that have given insights into the pre-Christian religion and has made important contributions in several other related fields.</li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inga_Maria_Mulk&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Inga Maria Mulk (page does not exist)">Inga Maria Mulk</a>  (1950–present) She has published several important papers on archaeology, historical geography, ethnographic studies etc., and is a Lule sami speaker.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Israel_Ruong" title="Israel Ruong">Israel Ruong</a>  (1903–1986) Born in Arjeplog. A Swedish-Sámi linguist, politician and professor of Sámi languages and culture at the University of Uppsala in Sweden. Israel Ruong spoke Pite Sámi as his mother tongue.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ande_Somby" class="mw-redirect" title="Ande Somby">Ande Somby</a>  (1958–present) Born in Buolbmat. A University Researcher, artist, cofounder of DAT.</li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Marechal&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jacqueline Marechal (page does not exist)">Jacqueline Marechal</a>  (1987–present) Born in Calgary. Geologist.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Explorers_and_adventurers">Explorers and adventurers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: Explorers and adventurers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Samuel_Balto" title="Samuel Balto">Samuel Balto</a> (1861–1921), Arctic explorer—one of the first people to cross Greenland on skis (together with Nansen)—and gold miner. The very famous dog <a href="/wiki/Balto" title="Balto">Balto</a> was named after Samuel Balto.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lars_Monsen" title="Lars Monsen">Lars Monsen</a> (1963–present) adventurer, explorer, journalist and author.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=68" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nils-Aslak-Valkeapaa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Nils-Aslak-Valkeapaa.jpg/170px-Nils-Aslak-Valkeapaa.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Nils-Aslak-Valkeapaa.jpg/255px-Nils-Aslak-Valkeapaa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Nils-Aslak-Valkeapaa.jpg/340px-Nils-Aslak-Valkeapaa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="430" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nils-Aslak-Valkeapaa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nils-Aslak_Valkeap%C3%A4%C3%A4" title="Nils-Aslak Valkeapää">Nils-Aslak Valkeapää</a>, a Sámi writer, musician and artist from Finland</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ella_Holm_Bull" title="Ella Holm Bull">Ella Holm Bull</a> (1929–2006),  author, musician, schoolteacher.</li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anders_Fjellner&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Anders Fjellner (page does not exist)">Anders Fjellner</a> (1795–1876), <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> priest and poet. Wrote down the mythological <a href="/wiki/Joik" title="Joik">joik</a> that inspired the Sámi flag.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ailo_Gaup_(author)" title="Ailo Gaup (author)">Ailo Gaup</a> (1944–2014), an author and neo-<a href="/wiki/Shaman" class="mw-redirect" title="Shaman">shaman</a> who participated in founding the <a href="/wiki/Beaivv%C3%A1%C5%A1_S%C3%A1mi_Theatre" class="mw-redirect" title="Beaivváš Sámi Theatre">Beaivváš Sámi Theatre</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Isak_Saba" title="Isak Saba">Isak Mikal Saba</a> (1875–1925), politician and writer. Was the first Sami parliamentarian (Norwegian Labour Party) and wrote the Sami national anthem.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Olaus_Sirma" title="Olaus Sirma">Olaus Sirma</a> (1655–1719), the first Sámi poet known by name.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Johan_Turi" title="Johan Turi">Johan Turi</a> (1854–1936), wrote first secular book in Sámi.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nils-Aslak_Valkeap%C3%A4%C3%A4" title="Nils-Aslak Valkeapää">Nils-Aslak Valkeapää</a> (1943–2001), musician, poet and artist.</li>
<li>Gladys Koski Holmes (1932–2005), a Sami-American artist, writer, and poet.  Holmes won poetry awards, published a children's book, and was the <a href="/wiki/Sami_Siida_of_North_America" title="Sami Siida of North America">Sami Siida of North America</a>'s ambassador to the Siida art show at the NANA festival in Tromsø.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_2">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=69" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Agnete_Johnsen,_ESC2016_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Agnete_Johnsen%2C_ESC2016_01.jpg/220px-Agnete_Johnsen%2C_ESC2016_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Agnete_Johnsen%2C_ESC2016_01.jpg/330px-Agnete_Johnsen%2C_ESC2016_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Agnete_Johnsen%2C_ESC2016_01.jpg/440px-Agnete_Johnsen%2C_ESC2016_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6016" data-file-height="4016" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Agnete_Johnsen,_ESC2016_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Agnete_Johnsen" title="Agnete Johnsen">Agnete Johnsen</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/%C3%81nde_Somby" title="Ánde Somby">Ánde Somby</a>, Sami musician and law professor.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adjagas" class="mw-redirect" title="Adjagas">Adjagas</a>, musical group.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mari_Boine" title="Mari Boine">Mari Boine</a> (1956–present), musician.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ane_Brun" title="Ane Brun">Ane Brun</a>, singer and songwriter.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fred_Buljo" title="Fred Buljo">Fred Buljo</a> (1988–present), rapper, singer, joik. Member of <a href="/wiki/KEiiNO" class="mw-redirect" title="KEiiNO">KEiiNO</a> and Duolva Duottar.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Frode_Fjellheim" title="Frode Fjellheim">Frode Fjellheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joik" title="Joik">joik</a> musician.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ingor_%C3%81nte_%C3%81ilo_Gaup" title="Ingor Ánte Áilo Gaup">Ingor Ánte Áilo Gaup</a> (1960–present), actor, composer, and folk musician.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sofia_Jannok" title="Sofia Jannok">Sofia Jannok</a> (1982–present), performer, musician and radio host.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Agnete_Johnsen" title="Agnete Johnsen">Agnete Johnsen</a> (1994–present), singer and songwriter.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Inga_Juuso" title="Inga Juuso">Inga Juuso</a> (1945–2014), singer and actress.</li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Kappfjell&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Gustav Kappfjell (page does not exist)">Gustav Kappfjell</a>  (1913–1999), Sami joiker and artist. Also noted for being part of the resistance movement during WW2.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Persen" title="John Persen">John Persen</a> (1941–2014), composer.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Joni_Mitchell" title="Joni Mitchell">Joni Mitchell</a> (1943–present), musician and painter.<sup id="cite_ref-nrk.no_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrk.no-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aikins_2005_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aikins_2005-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jaco_Pastorius" title="Jaco Pastorius">Jaco Pastorius</a> (1951–1987), influential American jazz musician, composer and electric bass player.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Roger_Pontare" title="Roger Pontare">Roger Pontare</a> (1951–present), musician.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wimme_Saari" title="Wimme Saari">Wimme Saari</a> (1959–present), musician.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lisa_Cecilia_Thomasson-Bosi%C3%B6" class="mw-redirect" title="Lisa Cecilia Thomasson-Bosiö">Lisa Cecilia Thomasson-Bosiö</a>, or Lapp-Lisa (1878–1932), singer.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nils-Aslak_Valkeap%C3%A4%C3%A4" title="Nils-Aslak Valkeapää">Nils-Aslak Valkeapää</a> (1943–2001), musician, poet and artist.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Niko_Valkeap%C3%A4%C3%A4" title="Niko Valkeapää">Niko Valkeapää</a> (1968–present), musician and songwriter.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Amoc_(rapper)" title="Amoc (rapper)">Mikkâl Morottaja</a> (1984–present), rap musician.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jonne_J%C3%A4rvel%C3%A4" title="Jonne Järvelä">Jonne Järvelä</a> (1974–present), musician and songwriter.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ulla_Pirttij%C3%A4rvi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulla Pirttijärvi">Ulla Pirttijärvi</a> (1971–present), <a href="/wiki/Joik" title="Joik">joik</a> singer.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/The_Blacksheeps" class="mw-redirect" title="The Blacksheeps">The Blacksheeps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">punk rock</a> band.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/%C3%98ystein_Aarseth" class="mw-redirect" title="Øystein Aarseth">Øystein Aarseth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_metal" title="Black metal">black metal</a> musician.</li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berit_Margrethe_Oskal&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Berit Margrethe Oskal (page does not exist)">Berit Margrethe Oskal</a> (1977–present), Sami joiker and musician.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vajas" title="Vajas">Vajas</a>, musical group.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_and_theatre">Film and theatre</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=70" title="Edit section: Film and theatre">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Beaivv%C3%A1%C5%A1_S%C3%A1mi_Theatre" class="mw-redirect" title="Beaivváš Sámi Theatre">Beaivváš Sámi Theatre</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NilsGaup.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/NilsGaup.jpg/170px-NilsGaup.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/NilsGaup.jpg/255px-NilsGaup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/NilsGaup.jpg/340px-NilsGaup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="5184" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NilsGaup.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nils_Gaup" title="Nils Gaup">Nils Gaup</a>, a Sámi film director from Norway</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mikkel_Gaup" title="Mikkel Gaup">Mikkel Gaup</a>, actor.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nils_Gaup" title="Nils Gaup">Nils Gaup</a> (1955–present), film director. Well-known films include <i><a href="/wiki/Ofela%C5%A1" class="mw-redirect" title="Ofelaš">Ofelaš</a></i> (<i>Pathfinder</i>), which was nominated for an Academy Award, and the 2008 film <i><a href="/wiki/Kautokeino-oppr%C3%B8ret_(2008)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kautokeino-opprøret (2008)">Kautokeino-Opprøret</a></i>, based on the <a href="/wiki/Sami_revolt_in_Guovdageaidnu" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami revolt in Guovdageaidnu">Kautokeino Rebellion</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sara_Margrethe_Oskal" title="Sara Margrethe Oskal">Sara Margrethe Oskal</a> (born 1970), actress, film director</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Anni-Kristiina_Juuso" title="Anni-Kristiina Juuso">Anni-Kristiina Juuso</a> (1979–present), actress.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lene_Cecilia_Sparrok" title="Lene Cecilia Sparrok">Lene Cecilia Sparrok</a> (1997), Norwegian actress of Sami descent.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tommy_Wirkola" title="Tommy Wirkola">Tommy Wirkola</a> (1979), Norwegian filmmaker of Finnish Sami descent.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lance_Henriksen" title="Lance Henriksen">Lance Henriksen</a> (1940), actor of Norwegian parentage; his grandmother was Sami.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9e_Zellweger" title="Renée Zellweger">Renée Zellweger</a> (1969), Oscar-winning actress whose Norwegian mother is of partial Sami descent.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Elle-M%C3%A1ij%C3%A1_Tailfeathers" title="Elle-Máijá Tailfeathers">Elle-Máijá Tailfeathers</a>, Canadian filmmaker, Indigenous rights activist, and actress of Sami and <a href="/wiki/Blackfoot_Confederacy" title="Blackfoot Confederacy">Blackfoot</a> heritage, on her father's and mother's sides, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Works in multiple genres including experimental, documentary, drama, and action.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics_and_society">Politics and society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=71" title="Edit section: Politics and society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lars_Levi_Laestadius" title="Lars Levi Laestadius">Lars Levi Laestadius</a> (1800–61), religious reformer, botanist and ethnologist.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ole_Henrik_Magga" title="Ole Henrik Magga">Ole Henrik Magga</a> (1947–present), politician. The first President of the Norwegian Sámi Parliament (NSR) and first Chairman of the UN Permanent Forum on Indigenous Issues.</li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nilla_Lansman&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Nilla Lansman (page does not exist)">Nilla Lansman</a> (1984–present), forester at <a href="/wiki/INSEAD" title="INSEAD">INSEAD</a>, the elite French business school</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Helga_Pedersen_(Norway)" title="Helga Pedersen (Norway)">Helga Pedersen</a> (1973–present) politician. The first Sami member of Government (Minister of Fisheries and Coastal Affairs, Norwegian Labour Party).<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Elsa_Laula_Renberg" title="Elsa Laula Renberg">Elsa Laula Renberg</a> (1877–1931), politician who among other things organized the first international Sami conference.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Isak_Saba" title="Isak Saba">Isak Mikal Saba</a> (1875–1925), politician and writer. Was the first Sami parliamentarian (Norwegian Labour Party) and wrote the Sami national anthem.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Janne_Seuruj%C3%A4rvi" title="Janne Seurujärvi">Janne Seurujärvi</a> (1975–present), politician. The first Sami member of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Finland" title="Parliament of Finland">Parliament of Finland</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_arts_2">Visual arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=72" title="Edit section: Visual arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hans_Ragnar_Mathisen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hans Ragnar Mathisen (page does not exist)">Hans Ragnar Mathisen</a>, artist.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Joni_Mitchell" title="Joni Mitchell">Joni Mitchell</a> (1943–present) musician and painter.<sup id="cite_ref-nrk.no_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrk.no-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aikins_2005_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aikins_2005-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nils-Aslak_Valkeap%C3%A4%C3%A4" title="Nils-Aslak Valkeapää">Nils-Aslak Valkeapää</a> (1943–2001), musician, poet and artist.</li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elsa_Bekkala&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Elsa Bekkala (page does not exist)">Elsa Bekkala</a> (1946–present), Painter, educator.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%A1mi_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=73" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1pmi_national_football_team" class="mw-redirect" title="Sápmi national football team">Sápmi national football team</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anja_P%C3%A4rson_Semmering_2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Anja_P%C3%A4rson_Semmering_2008.jpg/170px-Anja_P%C3%A4rson_Semmering_2008.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Anja_P%C3%A4rson_Semmering_2008.jpg/255px-Anja_P%C3%A4rson_Semmering_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Anja_P%C3%A4rson_Semmering_2008.jpg/340px-Anja_P%C3%A4rson_Semmering_2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anja_P%C3%A4rson_Semmering_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Anja_P%C3%A4rson" title="Anja Pärson">Anja Pärson</a> a Sámi skier from Sweden</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bsalming.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Bsalming.jpg/170px-Bsalming.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Bsalming.jpg/255px-Bsalming.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Bsalming.jpg/340px-Bsalming.jpg 2x" data-file-width="834" data-file-height="1356" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bsalming.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/B%C3%B6rje_Salming" title="Börje Salming">Börje Salming</a>, a retired ice hockey defenceman.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Leo_Komarov" title="Leo Komarov">Leo Komarov</a> (1987–present), <a href="/wiki/Finnish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Finnish people">Finnish</a> <a href="/wiki/Ice_Hockey" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice Hockey">Ice Hockey</a> player for the <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Maple_Leafs" title="Toronto Maple Leafs">Toronto Maple Leafs</a>. Born in <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> and raised in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> and is of <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russian</a>-Sami parentage.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ailo_Gaup_(motocross_rider)" title="Ailo Gaup (motocross rider)">Ailo Gaup</a> (1980–present), a motorcross sportsman who invented the "underflip".</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Morten_Gamst_Pedersen" title="Morten Gamst Pedersen">Morten Gamst Pedersen</a> (1981–present), <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">Football</a> player (former player for <a href="/wiki/Blackburn_Rovers_FC" class="mw-redirect" title="Blackburn Rovers FC">Blackburn Rovers</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/B%C3%B6rje_Salming" title="Börje Salming">Börje Salming</a> (1951–present), legendary <a href="/wiki/NHL" class="mw-redirect" title="NHL">NHL</a> defenseman, member of <a href="/wiki/Hockey_Hall_of_Fame" title="Hockey Hall of Fame">Hockey Hall of Fame</a>, voted to the <a href="/wiki/IIHF" class="mw-redirect" title="IIHF">IIHF</a> all-century team.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Anja_P%C3%A4rson" title="Anja Pärson">Anja Pärson</a> (1981–present) and <a href="/wiki/Jens_Byggmark" title="Jens Byggmark">Jens Byggmark</a> (1985–present), alpine skiers.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jon_R%C3%B8nningen" title="Jon Rønningen">Jon Rønningen</a>, wrestler. Olympic gold medalist.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lars_R%C3%B8nningen" title="Lars Rønningen">Lars Rønningen</a>, wrestler.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Halvorsen" title="John Halvorsen">John Halvorsen</a>, athletics</li></ul>
